Impatient Housewife (to messenger): “What
Baking Powder is this you have
brought me ? ”
Messenger : “That’s what was given me.”
Impatient Housewife (excitedly) : “ Then
take it back, and ask for—
BATHO’S Powder, for, you know.
That other brands deceive them
With “ ads ” and jokes that pass as smokes,
They tempt you to believe them.
But oft my thoughts again repeat,
That BATHOS Powder is the best,
And I know it can’t be beat.”

“THE BULLETIN.”
(Published at SYDNEY , N.S.W.)
The Unique Weekly—Australia’s Nouparel'
Illustrated Newspaper.
“The Bulletin,”
Premier Australian Literary Journal,
Is the only paper which has a consistent and dis-
tinctive Australian National Policy.
The Bulletin favours—
A Republican Form of Government.
One Person, one Vote.
Complete Secularisation and Freedom of State
Education.
Reform of the Criminal Code and Prison
System.
A United Australia and Protection against
the World.
Australia for the Australians. —The cheap
Chinaman, the cheap nigger, and the
cheap European pauper to be absolutely
excluded.
A State Bank, the issue of bank-notes to be a
State monopoly.
The direct election of Ministers by Parlia-
ment, instead of Party Government or
rather Government by Contradiction.
A new Parliamentary System - one House to
be elected by constituencies as at present ;
the other to be chosen by the whole
country voting as one Constituency.
A Universal System of Compulsory Life
Insurance.
The entire Abolition of the Private Owner-
ship of Land.
The Referendum.
The Abolition of Titles of so-called “ nobility. ’
The public eye rejects as uninteresting more
than half of what appears in the newspapers of
the day. It is only the remainder that is found
in The Bulletin.
The Bulletin is an aggressive Democratic
paper which strives to exclude from its artisti-
cally-condensed columns all matter that is not
of general human interest. The Bulletin was
started 13 years ago with no capital but brains,
and has become the representative Australian
journal, because it possesses a vitality lacked
by the countless news paper- ventures in which
Australian capitalists have unavailingly spent
large fortunes.
The Bulletin’s conductors claim that every
progressive paper on the Australian continent has
more or less endeavoured to profit by its example
and follow its lines. The result of its powerful
influence and its unprecedented literary and com-
mercial success can at once be seen by comparing
the methods pursued by the Australian daily and
weekly press prior to and immediately after th
first issue of The Bulletin.
From the date of The Bulletin’s inaugural
publication its success was assured. It had at
once a dozen more or, less servile imitators, many
of which even took, so far as was possible, its
name. They are now all dead, while. The Bul-
letin continues the one phenomenal success in
Australian journalism, the one completely non-
local and distinctly universal Australasian news-
paper.
The Bulletin proprietary have spent some
thousands of pounds in fitting their buildings
with the latest appliances for high-class printing
and the production of illustrations, and they have
now issued The Bulletin in an entirely new
form—printed with new type of improved legi-
bility on the finest- paper, and adorned with
sketches from the hands of the ablest and best-
paid newspaper artists Australia has ever seen.
The Bulletin is the only well-established Aus-
tralian journal which, throughout its career, has
been consistently Radical. It points with pride
to the fact that for years it stood alone among
secular newspapers in advocating the Irish
National Cause, and that it was the only Aus-
tralian journal which denounced the Soudan
Expedition from its inception.
The Bulletin is the beacon-fire of national
progress; it is the forceful summons to the
crusade against the monopolies in land, wealth,
power, and privilege—the direct cause of half the
misery which now afflicts British humanity.
Against the clauns of avaricious clergy who claim
to monopolise salvation, against the dark despotism
of grasping plutocrats, and against the exercise of
any human authority which has not been granted
by the people over whom it is exercised, The
Bulletin will fearlessly and ruthlessly contend.
The Bulletin, unlike the ordinary weekly
journal, is not loaded with a weight of matter
transferred from the columns of a daily paper
issued from the same office. The Bulletin is
not connected with any other paper, and the
matter it contains is all specially prepared by its
own unequalled staff.
The Bulletin is the journal which, of all Aus-
tralian journals, is most frequently quoted in Eng-
land, America, and the colonies.
The Bulletin, which is the only paper sold
by runners in the streets of every Australasian
capital, is the most widely-circulated of all papers
published south of the equator, and is the only
Australasian publication which enjoys a popu-
larity unhampered by the political boundaries
that so closely restrict other journals to the
colonies in which they are printed.
The Bulletin’s red cover is equally familiar
to the bushman of the Far North, the stockman
of Central Australia, the pearl-sheller of Torres
Straits, and the digger in the Maori land
Ranges. A paper which is at once the most
popular city publication and the organ of the
intelligent bushman must indeed be broadly based.
Perhaps the strongest proof of The Bulletin’s
popularity is found in the unchallengeable fact—
easily ascertainable from any postal official—that,
of all journals issued in Australasia, The Bul-
letin is the one most frequently remailed from
one reader to another.
The Bulletin is going ahead faster than any
other paper, because its vast mass of original and
specially-written reading matter is the brightest
and best, its illustrations the cleverest and most
artistic, and its policy the boldest and most
straightforward ever submitted to the Australian
public.

order, to be called the Star of Australasia. Cable.

SATURDAY, JANUARY 27, 1894.
The “ Star of Australasia and Canada.”
Another order of knighthood—the “ Star
of Australasia and Canada ” —is vaguely sug-
gested as an addition to the existing varieties
of “colonial” nobility. The ostensible
author of this suggestion is the Marquis of
■Lohne, Victoria’s reddish and bony son-
in-law, chiefly known to fame as the per-
petrator of a new and infamously bumpy
metrical version of the Psalms. According
to Lorne’s theory, the proposed “ honour ”
will not even profess to be given for general
merit, or for public services rendered to the
honoured one’s own province ; it is to be
baldly and undisguisedly a reward for, and
given to, the cause of Imperial Federation—
and for no other purpose. From this pro-
position it would appear that Lorne’s diplo-
macy is as rude and primitive as, and even
more bumpy than, his version of the Psalms.
His proposition gives away the royal family
badly! It proves, in the first place, that
royalty is deeply anxious that Imperial
Federation should come to pass, and that
alone is sufficient to make the democracy
regard it with even more suspicion than
heretofore. The heavy-jowlcd House of
Guelph is distinguished by such cast-iron
Toryism, and is so incurably Avodded to the
Avays of its small, podgy, despotic ancestors
of Hanover, that its approval is calculated
to blast any political project, however pro-
mising. A brighter politician than Lorne
or his brother-in-law, Wales, would there-
fore have studiously concealed his sentiments
on the subject, and not have cursed an
already feeble project with the dead Aveight
of the royal approval.
» « •
The proposed Order has other disadvantages.
As already mentioned, it is to be given for
services in the cause of Imperial Federation,
and for them only. No other single cause
has ever been propped up in such a fashion,
and this insidious distinction plainly implies
that Imperial Federation needs more violent
extraneous assistance than any other im-
perial fad of the day. It is the feeblest
and most abjectly tottersomo of the
lot, and requires to bo shored up
with an order of knighthood all to itself
in order to keep it reasonably alive.
Either that, or else the fact that even the
disreputable and ragged decoration of St.
Michael and St. George, by which thebreasts
of so many shady characters are adorned,
is supposed to be a reward for some kind of
political merit, makes it necessary to provide
a separate place of refuge for a crowd which
has no merit at all, and which cannot, with
any decency, bo put among the existent
K.O.M.G.’s lest they should be depreciated
and rendered unsaleable. Or, as a third
alternative, it may be that even the ordinary
groveller will not be an Imperial Federa-
tionist so long as there is any possibility of
getting a title by other means, and, there-
fore, it is proposed to create a dignity which
can’t be reached by any other means in order
to goad him to this outrage upon the last
semblance of his intelligence. In other
words, it may be that the Star of Aus-
tralasia and Canada is intended to be a
specially lofty honour, and the reason of its
proposed existence is that no politician cares
to be an Imperial Federationist at the
present low figure, on the ground that there
is too much obloquy for the money and that
the reward is not commensurate with the
doubts cast on his reasoning faculties.
» # #
Imperial Federation has all along been a
purely artificial enthusiasm. Nobody save the
British royal family, and Parkin, and a few
other interested parties ever viewed it in a
serious light, and the suggestion that poli-
ticians should be paid to view it in a serious
light shows distinctly that its prospects are
not improving. The scheme of the projected
“ Order ” is an eloquent commentary on the
awful stupidity which grows around a throne.
It acknowledges, in the first place, that it is
necessary to buy up the politicians of the
“colonies,” in order to induce them to lend
a hand at galvanising the old, dead idea into
new life, and it assumes, in the same breath,
that the sort of politician who can be openly
bought is likely to be of some use in the galva-
nising process. The cause is hopelessly un-
popular, and, therefore, the man who takes it
up is to receive a gewgaw as compensation for
the scoffs and the aged eggs of the con-
temptuous multitude, and the danger of
ejectment from office, and the other disad-
vantages attached to the Imperial creed. But
here the scheme degenerates into idiocy, for
the Imperial Federation enthusiast, when
once he has gained his title, will have no
inducement to expose himself to public
derision any longer, and every man who is
adorned with the Star of Australasia and
Canada will be, to all intents and purposes,
a Federationist lost. And if the bestowal
of the Order is postponed until the old age
of the recipient, so as to keep him steady in
his faith so long as possible, it will presently
be discovered that, though the knighthood
may come in the end, political extinction
comes a great deal faster. According to
Lorne’s great project, Federal enthusiasm
is to be openly a matter of business calcula-
tion, and the calculation works out very
badly either way.
Further, the scheme altogether ignores the
obvious fact that, when once the system
of paying the enthusiastic Federationist
for his enthusiasm is openly adopted, the
only hope of accomplishing Federation is
to buy up a national majority straight
away. Even if the Crown gets the
Imperial Federationist that it pays for,
which is exceedingly doubtful, the fact
that he is paid for joining the cause will
prevent him fetching any large following
along with him. Therefore, each Federa-
tionist will have to be bought separately,
with a serious risk of non-delivery of the
article when it has been bought. The
“ colonial” knight of the past was paid, as a
rule, for doing nothing ; any politician who
rose to power and used his power to keep
things as they were, and to prevent the pro-
gress of Democratic legislation, could always
hope for a “dignity ” sooner or latter. Or,
if the “dignity” was slow in coming and
seemed to have been shipped by gravel-train
or by protracted raft, he could generally
hasten its arrival by urging on Democratic
measures, and refusing to stop his wild
career without compensation. It is now
proposed, however, that two orders of
“colonial” knighthood shall exist side by
side, one of which is to be gained by doing
nothing as heretofore—by simply existing in a
public capacity, and being one’s own contemp-
tible self ; while the other, of seemingly no
greater value, is to be attained by rushing
violently down a steep place into the sea, in
the hope that the dignity may arrive before
the patient arrives with a fatal concussion at
the bottom.
# * •
This discrepancy shows that the Crown
utterly fails to realise the depth and breadth
and vastness of the public indifference to
Imperial Federation and everything con-
nected therewith. The politician who applies
himself to earning the star of the proposed
“Order” must set out resolutely to bo a
bore, a weariness, and a public nuisance.
He must talk and write with a horrid itera-
tion about a subject concerning which the
nation takes no more interest than it does in
the existence or non-existence of boot-jacks
in the moon. He must bo an agony, a
general misery, and a monomaniac, and
when he rises to speak he must be content
to see the audience rush for the door to get
away from the horror of his eloquence.
Australasia, in recent years, has only raised
one man who devoted himself to the ques-
tion with sufficient enthusiasm to qualify for
a knighthood—the awful Mortimer Frank-
lyn, who, after spending £70,000, more or
less, of other people’s money, hypothecated
his watch and went bankrupt in his efforts to
overcome the public indifference, and finally
abandoned the country in despair without
rousing one vestige of interest in the subject.
Considering the greatness of his fall, no
politician who has anything to lose or any
watch to pawn is ever likely to follow
his example with much success. When Aus-
tralasia couldn’t find food and clothing and
jewellery and garish raiment for even one
enthusiastic Imperial Federationist, the pros-
pect of it ever supporting enough to make a
decent order of knighthood is hardly worth
mentioning.
/ * * *
The Crown’s faith in knighthoods as a
means of bribery and corruption is a touch-
ing circumstance. They have done good
service as a means of inducing a few men to
block the public sentiment by means of
Parliamentary forms, but when it is pro-
posed that they should bo employed to
create a new public sentiment it is alto-
gether too large an order. It is im-
possible to distribute enough knighthoods to
buy a popular majority without making the
blessed “ decoration ” even more ridiculous
than it is at present. The system wants
inverting to make it serviceable. If “ Mr.”
were the highest dignity in the empire, and
knighthood was a degradation to be inflicted
by a police magistrate on drunks and persons
of bad character; and the various degrees
of nobility, down to the awful disgrace
of a dukedom—which would be reserved for
persons sentenced to long terras of penal
servitude—were applied in a similar fashion,
then the Crown could bring a solid influence
to bear upon the great mass of the com-
munity. For then the bulk of the nation
would already occupy the highest rank of
nobility, and if the “disloyal” section of it was
in constant danger of being degraded into the
peerage it could, be kept in a state of reason-
able submission. Failingthis something might
be done in the cause of Imperial Federation
by offering a large hunk of bread and cheese,
as a plain, satisfying inducement, to each
individual supporter. And, failing this also,
it seems as if the solitary Imperial Federa-
tionist must keep on hypothecating his watch
on behalf of a lost cause as heretofore ; and
the Imperial Federation League, which closed
up so dismally a month or two ago, must
remain closed; and the gaudy Imperial
Institute must pursue its wild career as a
nigger-minstrel show to the bitter end.
PLAIN ENGLISH.
An Instructive “Awakening.”
The “beautiful end ” of Mrs. Knorr, who was
hanged last week in the joyful hope of a resur-
rection, will assist to explain the meaning of
that peace —call it “ aggressive peace ” —which
the typical Hardshell finds at prayer meetings.
Most of us scoff at the smug pietist who,
living a life of chicanery and holy uncharitable-
ness, takes every available opportunity to
mention how nice he feels since he was saved.
We scoff at what we consider the blasphemous
impudence of the lank-haired creature, assum-
ing that he really knows himself for what he is,
and is eonsciously trying to impose upon irre-
ligious humanity. In truth, he is generally im-
posing upon himself. The converted grocer,
who sandeth his sugar and never breaketh
bread until he hath asked a blessing upon it,
belongs to the evangelical world of self-
deception. A fraudulent sense of comfort, a
warm, though utterly bogus, feeling of “grace”
pervades the inner consciousness of the snuffling
saint, and, to all earthly intents and purposes,
he enjoys “ the peace of God which passeth
all understanding.” This gift of self-
deception seems to be confined to beings of the
lower order of intellect, and constitutional
tricksters. No brainy person, educated to a
regard for any good principles, can overcome
conscience. He or she may arrange terms with
conscience, and feel tolerably easy in the thought
that the debt of sin may be ultimately squared
by a farthing in the £ composition, but the
complacency of the grocer can never grease the
countenance of an intelligent thinker. Nor is
the genuine oil of imaginary salvation to be
seen upon the faces of Jabez Balfour and ten
thousand other bold financial swindlers who
merely pretend to snuffle, being too crafty to
get hoodwinked by themselves. These be the
confessed hypocrites, the Dr. Cantwells of
chapelism, and to class them with their
admiring Maw worms is to libel both parties.
Between the vile wretch who quotes Scrip-
ture with his tongue in his cheek, and the
objectionable insect who registers himself
as a “child of Christ” (on the strength
of a certain “ awakening ” which was the hol-
lowest mental delusion) there is a difference
uhpcrceived by casual observers. Mrs. Knorr,
the murderess, is a light to lighten this dark
distinction. Her demise is an object-lesson in
the art of manufacturing the spurious Peace of
God that docs duty for salvation in snuffle
circles. A few hours of “ spiritual ” converse
and hymn-singing with a gaol-chaplain were
sufficient to persuade this pitiless baby-slayer
that she had found grace, in proof of which
pleasing conviction she died “as game as a
pebble,” after twanging forth an abominably
maudlin ditty, expressive of her everlasting
safety. If the Peace of God —a noble term,
never lightly used by a non-church-goer—
inspired Mrs. Knorr to sing Sankey’s hymn,
then It would indeed be past all understanding.
What the cold-hearted profligate did find in
her dungeon-cell was the peace of the grocer,
the grace of Mawworm, the vulgar counterfeit
“comfort” that, being constantly hawked
about as a Heavenly attribute, makes sinners
look with suspicion upon their own too rare
consciousness of having done a good action, and
fills them with a horrid dread of feeling all the
better for it.
Farkes on the “Constitution."
N.S.W. Minister Slattery’s letter in last Mon-
day’s Sydney Telegraph is another lament-
able specimen of the kind of thing which always
will keep happening to dear old Farkes. That
aged beguiler has been complaining with a loud
and fearsome shriek of the conduct of the Dibbs
Cabinet in holding office with a majority of only
one—not counting the Speaker, and has
heaved all the Billingsgate in his dictionary
at G. Republican Dibbs in consequence.
Also, the old man objects to Dibbs’
“ haction ” in previously getting a prorogation
in the face of a hostile vote. And now
Slattery, in his own beastly, unpleasant
fashion, points out that Mr. Henry Farkes, in
1866, didn’t resign on two occasions when the
Ministry hadn’t even a fair majority of one, and
was saved only by the Speaker’s casting vote ;
that he kept on holding office after two
decisive defeats; and that in 1868 he
was saved again by the Speaker’s cast-
ing vote, and grabbed his billet all
the same. Then, in 1874, Premier Farkes,
being defeated on a motion of censure by one vote
prorogued Parliament just as Dibbs did last
year, and held on to Ids portfolio like a drown-
ing man grasping at a cheque. Next session
another vole of censure was defeated only by
the casting vote of the Speaker, and Farkes
prorogued Parliament again and held on to his
job with his teeth as heretofore. Then, being
defeated yet again, he resigned, and almost im-
mediately afterwards he had the brassy insol-
ence to move that John Robertson’s con-
duct, in not resigning after a defeat, was
“subversive to the principles of the Constitoo-
shun.” A month later ParkEst^jwas in office
again, and a Vote of censure being moved, was
once more saved by one vote—and didn’t resign,
as usual. And two months further on the same
hungry howler was defeated by two votes and
declined to take any notice of the circumstance,
whereupon he was defeated again by six votes
and still didn’t budge until he failed to obtain
a dissolution and was unable to get supply,
when he reluctantly crawled down. This sort
of thing is painful reading, and will probably
cause Farkes to rise at his next meeting and
state, in his little squeaky voice, that Slattery
is a loathsome tarantula, or something equally
bright and irrelevant.
Copeland’s Downfall.
The Bulletin is glad to hear that in the
opinion of the great bulk of the N.S.W.
Labour-party, the episode which led to the
formal resignation of N.S.W. Lands-Minister
Copeland reflects little glory upon the sawn-
off Cold Teg, apostle who brought the trouble
about. Conceymiig Copeland’s foolishness
there is, of course, no manner of doubt. Years
ago he got a solemn warning against too much
hilariousness and flow of soul, when, late one
night, at a public dinner following a day’s severe
outing, a mean little pressman~took down a
particularly enthusiastic speech of his verbatim,
with the loose enthusiasm inserted at full
length, and a particularly mean party-paper
cold-bloodedly printed it in all its incoherency
for purely party purposes. The effect of the
warning, however, seems to have evaporated,
and Copeland this time, as much under the
influence of rage as of soda-water, has, in a
measure, repeated the offence. However, the
shaft aimed at him has not only failed to kill its
intended victim but has actually boomeranged
on to the thrower. Between the bluff, big-
hearted, full-blooded, obstinate pioneer miner,
and the little, virtuous bagman who got away
by the side-door while his waiting enemy raged
around the front entrance, the manly portion of
the public has been quick to judge. Copeland
is going to live politically because the public
somehow revolts from the spectacle of a brainy
veteran with a long, clean record and a 50-inch
chest, being executed by a pallid draper’s
drummer who has yet to make a solid reputation
in any other line than (say) ladies’ underclothing
as 465. lid. per gross. In the public mind, the
draper belongs to the same sex as the curate,
and the man who lifts his hand in anger to a
draper is unworthy of the name he bears. The
fact that Copeland did attempt to lay violent
hands on his softgoods antagonist is the most
melancholy feature in the case, bub doubtless
Thou Shalt not Bow to the Ancient Lie.
(FOR THE BULLETIN.)
If faith thou lack bend not thy back, bow not
thy head to the holy hymn,
Although it swell as a golden bell and die in the
vaulted distance dim;
Take not the tale of a priestling pale for the
god-like Truth that is throned on high,
With head unbowed in the cringing crowd,
thou shall not bend to the Sacred Lie.
And if thou love, all else above, the peerless
pearl of the People’s Cause,
Thou wilt despise each paltry prize and brave
the ban of the leprous laws,;
1 hough the base-born brood of Power and Pelf
in princely chariots pass thee by,
With head unbowed in the cringing crowd thou
shall not bend to the Gilded Lie.
If thou be free as a man should be—if hate thou
bear to the tyrant band,
Reject each gaud, each gilded fraud, each
bauble gag in the royal hand ;
For the breath of a king is a blighting blast—
it blows, and the flowers of Freedom die,
With head unbowed in the cringing crowd thou
shall not bend to the Kingly Lie.
Thinkest thou the dead whose hero-bed is oft
bedewed with a nation’s tears
Inscribed their name on the Roll of Fame by a
life that limped through a lane of fears ?
Nay ! Nay ! With a fearless front and free
with a noble.fire in the flashing eye,
They never bowed with the cringing crowd—■
they never bent to the Ancient Lie.

P. LUFTIG.

A rumour that the Patterson Government
intended to pitchfork Tom Bent into the chair"
manship of Vic. Railways had some founda"
tion in fact, and may be a possible job even now
The idea of such an appointment is outrageous
enough from a national point of view, but
a country which elects the modern Victorian
Parliament, and a press that abases itself at the
boots of Patterson, have no claim to take a
national view of anything. The Bulletin
would rejoice in the spectacle of Bent waving
the banner of “Reform, Retrenchment, and
High Commercial Principle” at the railways,
but for the impossibility of retaining him as
Speaker at the same time. Speaker Bent is a
most desirable institution for reasons not
generally appreciated. Bent, as Speaker and
Railway Chairman combined, might prove the
salvation of Victoria by causing the body
politic to wake up suddenly and vomit. Until
Victoria has vomited it will remain in a bad
way.
♦
The result of the by-election at Townsville
(N.Q.), though anticipated, is significant. It
emphasises that general hostility of North
Queensland to the existing Government which
was a leading feature at last year’s general
election. The contest took place on a definite
issue between two candidates only. Ogden,
the Labour representative defeated at the
previous election, had in his favour en-
thusiasm -and good organisation. Will-
mett, the Ministerialist, though his sup-
porters were divided by petty jealousies,
commanded greater esteem as an old
and respectable resident, who had done
much for the Separation cause which both
candidates supported. The struggle was thus
fairly equal, and the fact that, under such cir-
cumstances, Ogden should obtain the substan-
tial majority of 184, clearly shows that, ably
led and discreetly counselled, the Democratic
party in North Queensland may carry all before
them ; for Townsville is by no means so Radical
as the inland mining centres.
Dear Bulletin, —This morning, in London,
the thermometer was 28°, and of course the
frost was big. To keep myself warm I went
to the Imperial Institute —and found a bigger
frost l —about 2°, I should think. New South
Wales is represented by a portrait in oils of
Palinurus Hegellii. At first I thought it was
George Republican D. boiled down, but
closer inspection revealed a Port Jack-
son crawfish! flanked by six bottles of
red - mullet oil from Port Jackson, and
six bottles of full-bodied, 36 0.p., Muscat
from down Albury way. The crawfish smiles
down on Sir Somers Vine’s visitors and
intimates in the volapuk of the crustacean that
he has the honour, &c., of representing Sydney
Harbour, one of the brightest gems in the
British Crown. (Cheers.) What a farce ! The
industries of New South Wales are represented
by a miserable collection of rubbish which
would disgrace the saloon of the Presbyterian
Church South Sea mission schooner ! Who is
responsible ? P.S.—The Imperial Institute has
nearly cost £500,000 now, and is empty.
A Victorian correspondent to The Bulletin :
Dear Bulletin,— lie the evergreen question of Bible
literature in State schools, it is pretty certain that if this
Branch of learning is entirely excluded from the curricu-
lum, the gap will not he filled up in after life. At best,
there is not even a negative bliss in such ignorance, and
I’ll just give you an instance of what may happen to any
little isolated community whose Bible is “a disreputable
foreign paper with a red cover.” During the wretched
iuiumu of ’B2 I was loafing for currajong on one of the
largest stations in Upper Riverina. One day, going
icross to the homestead, I met a narangy in the horse-
paddock. “ I say, Collins,” he broke out; “ who were
the daughters of Moab ? ” “ New sell ?” I asked, know-
ing my man. "No ; honour bright; I’d give a note, if I
had it, to find out.” Having been brought up at the feet of
Gamaliel, I was able to satisfy the young fellow. Near
the siockyard a rouseabout intercepted me; “ Boss wants
you, Collins ; he’s in the store. But, I say, do you know
who the deuce the daughters of Moab was ? ” I
supplied the information, and hastened to the
store: “Good day, Collins. Able to keep your frames
alive ? By the way, do you know who the daughters of
Moab were, and how they distinguished themselves?” I
satisfied the manager’s thirst for knowledge, and passed
on. The blacksmith waylaid me at the forge: "Just
the man I wanted to see! Do you know who,” &c., &c.
Again I narrated the incident of Baal-Peor, and, musing
on the touch of ignorance which made the whole station
kin, went to the hut to get a drink of tea and a bit of
brownie. “Good shot!” said the cook, as I entered.
“I been watchin’ for you this fortnit. Do you know
who,” &c., &o. Once more I paiaphrased into the
extremely vulgar tongue that xxv. chapter of Numbers ;
then the cook enlightened me as to the cause of this new-
born interest in sacred lore -placing a few consecutive
copies of a Sydney paper before me to explain his
explanation. The first paper reported a bazaar
held in aid of one of the Sydney churches; and
the correspondence columns contained a letter from
a clergyman of the same church, wherein
the rev. gent. anticipating and out - Sledding
Stead— coolly stigmatised the bazaar-attendants as
" daughters of Moab.” As might be expected, the cor-
respondence columns of the following issue overflowed
with angry protest from the fathers, brothers and friends
of the bazaar gills. The next issue, again, contained a
sullen semi-apology from the unclean saint, whose cloth,
it appeared, had failed to protect him from threats of
personal violence. “Naturally,” continued the cook,
“ we was in a devil of a state to find out who these parties
was; and we hadn’t a ghost of a Bible on the station.
They got one on Wircheliba, but the Somersets won’t
lend it, and they can’t find the place themselves.” This
is merely to show that the Bible is not the secret of
Riverina's greatness. Indeed, a friend of mine—a
boundary man named soihething like Angus M'Piierson
attributes the deficiency of rainfall throughout Riverina
to the neglect of Scripture in that region, and proves his
contention by quoting Scotland as a contrast. Be this as
it may, there is no doubt that the healthy recoil from
priestcraft is carrying us too far. The Bible-hater is no
less irrational than the Bible-faddist. The O.T. ia the
most interesting, instructive, and authentic section of
ancient history within the range of literature, and an in-
exhaustible mine of fearless philosophy and sublime fore-
cast. The N.T., rightly read, will be the text-book of
ideal Socialism when its professional perverter is more
extinct and less regretted than the Tasmanian black-
fellow. In the interests of moral progress, the Bible must
be read ; and in the interests of honest interpretation,
the parson must go.— Tom Collins. /
A member of N.S.W. Labour-party in Parlia-
ment :
Our troubles arise from- ‘he fact that the central com-
mittee of the electoral league is, and has been, “ bossed ”
by men who are more occupied in attempting to gain
seats in Parliament for themselves than in honest endea-
vour to swell the ranks of the party in the House. Every
one of these men, to my mind, is incapacitated for office
on that committee by the mere fact that they are candi-
dates. Their unfitness to run such a concern has been
otherwise shown times without number. For instance,
when they voted themselves on to the league conference by
virtue of their office. Further, 1 may say that they have
shamefully abused their trust by keeping no books and
furnishing no balance-sheet until forced by an accession of
new blood. Throughout they worked, not to break fresh
ground and win new seats, but to discredit and under-
mine the pioneer members of the party, without excep-
tion, in order to take their places. The treatment of Mr.
G. D. Clark at Leichhardt is a case in point.
That member is right in the main. No man
can be a competent judge in his own cause ;
and no man who is a central committeeman of
any electoral organisation should himself be a
candidate, secretly or openly, for Parliament.
No man should be elected to such a committee
who doesn’t bind himself to abstain from Par-
liamentary candidature for (say) a year or two
after ceasing to be a committeeman.
The latest oration of Miss Jessie Ackerman,
the returned social-purity priestess, conclu-
sively shows that her travels have not improved
her intellect. This lady is a good type of the
excellent people who are continually trying to
put a quart into a pint-pot. She thoroughly
believes in making the world moral by Act of
Parliament. Her panacea is, in the first place,
legislation, and, in the second place, legislation,
and, in the third place, legislation. She is
convinced that the abolition of the social evil,
of the drink traffic, and of crime in general is
only a matter of placing so many laws, in so
many chapters, and so many clauses, upon the
statute-book. No argument will persuade
the Miss Ackermans of the world that a
stream cannot rise higher than its source;
or that, if the laws of the community prescribe
a standard of virtue above the common practice
of the community, those laws are so much waste
paper. The appeal to history is wasted. They
cannot realise that a change in the people must
precede a change in the mode of government,
if the latter is to be effectual; they have not
the least idea that reform, to be permanent,
must begin at the bottom and not at the top.
Doubtless Miss Ackerman means well ; but it
is a pity she does not know a little better,
A Melbourne daily says : “ Of all the applica-
tions received for the position of hangman, the
most painful was that of a man who had been
an officer in the Imperial Army, and whose
father before him had been an eminent soldier.”
Why was this case a whit more distressing than
any other ? Impecunious ex-officers are to be
found in thousands, loafing, sponging, swind-
ling, and generally giving themselves prepos-
terous airs. Asa body they are the most
useless creatures on God’s earth. It is a
slightly redeeming feature about the trained
man-killer under discussion that he would like
to get the hangman’s job. He is ready to earn
his own living. He has been bred and educated
to regard human slaughter as an excellent Tory
institution, and the eminent warrior, his papa,
doubtless taught him to lament the abolition
of wholesale hanging and flogging in the army
he adorned. Asa matter of fact, this person’s
application was the least unnatural, and there-
fore the most excusable, of any that came to
hand, and The Bulletin herewith calls upon
Premier Patterson to give the gallant colonel
a trial. Let him have the higher-class hang-
ings, at any rate, and then the dom’d cwiminal
of taste will be able to say that he fell like a
soldier, at the hands of an officer and gentle-
man, haw 1

The law, as represented at Melbourne City
Police Court, is making a specially asinine
attempt to reform a damsel named Kiley.
This unfortunate wretch was originally cap-
tured from a Chinese den and made into a
“ ward of the State,” that is to say, the State
gave the police a general order to keep on
capturing Kiley as often as they thought fit.
The Harmy took her in hand soon after this,
but the Ward’s taste for prayer was blunted,
and one day she slunk silently away in her
poke bonnet and married a Chinaman, Mrs.
Kiley, her mamma, being present at the
solemn rites. Then the Harmy matron, hearing
that the new-made bride was “ living under
most abandoned conditions,” persuaded the
police to drag her from her lawful Chow, and
bring her up before the Court. The Court
sentenced her to rejoin the Harmy. At latest
dates this young married woman was being
conveyed back to “barracks,” or somewhere,
in a cab. She was tugging violently at two
constables and protesting that she preferred
prison to Salvation, but the State heeded not
her cries or kicks. It goes without saying that
the abandoned angel will presently regain her
freedom and go back like the prodigal to the
home of her opium-eater to resume domestic
duties, whereupon the Harmy and the State will
take measures as before. Meanwhile, there
seems to be a screw loose in the State s
authority for kidnapping Mrs. Chung
Lung, nee Kiley. She is treated as a
domestic criminal, whilst her loving husband,
the partner of her abandonment, is simply
ignored in the matter. The fact that the Law
doesn’t arrest the Chow is presumptive evidence
that it has no right to annex the wife. Further-
more, the absurdity of placing a girl against
her will with people who can’t legally detain
her, is as apparent as the certainty that Chung
Lung is entitled to come along with his mam-
age-certificate and sue the Government for
depriving him of nuptial bliss.
Premier Patterson’s speech on Capital and
Labour, at South Melbourne, was a pitiful
exhibition. Interlarded with scriptural quota-
tions and slangy proverbs, the whole drivel
amounted to a declaration that, as Capital and
Labour were indispensable to each other, any
friction between employers and employed
meant ruin and desolation. This old wheeze,
coming from an assumedly intelligent person,
gave a fearful shock to the audience. r l hat
Patterson can’t see that Capital and Labour
are synonymous terms, and that it is Capitalism
which the world is sick of, and that money is
merely a form of wealth which artificial
arrangements have substituted for Capital, is
painfully evident by his lamentations over the
present dearth of cash in Victoria. Capital is
the active principle of any industrial process,
and when it (i.e., labour and machinery)' is
utilised for anti-social purposes, such as ex-
hausting the national energy by entertaining
royalty so that a few individuals may receive
special advantages therefrom, the less said
about the relationship of Capital to Labour by
any axe-grinding meat-men the better.
Despite the shyness of capitalists, the Q.
Government is doing its best to press forward
the construction of land-grant railways on the
iniquitous terms agreed to by the last corrupt
Parliament; and emissary Hume Black, who
sacrificed a number of first class convictions for
his travelling political scholarship of £IOOO a
year, is talking oa the subject to all in Britain
who will listen. If he talks long enough and
strong enough, and M‘llwraith is able to
collect coolies enough, there is no reason why
Queensland should not regret a land-grant
railway just as forcibly as West Australia
regrets hers at the present moment. Certainly,
the Queensland offer is most tempting. You
are paid in land for building the line, you run
it as long as it suits you, and then you hand it
to the Government and get paid over again in
cash. And, as there are no mining reservations,
there is the chance of a Broken Hill or a Mount
Morgan thrown in.
—_—
Dr. Paton, the Presbyterian missionary, has
been telling the British Secretary of State that
the Queensland Kanaka lives in “ scarcely dis-
guised slavery.” The mission Kanaka generally
lives in undisguised slavery, spending his life
in hewing wood and drawing water for a pack
of greasy professional saints, while an elaborate
system of fines punishes failure to attend
church, or to repeat the Ten Commandments
when called upon, or to pick out the best
cocoanuts to give to God. On the majority of
Queensland plantations the Kanaka is quite
as well off as he is under the thumb of the
missionary on his native island. The Kanaka
is offensive, not because he is badly treated, for
that isn’t always so; but because his employer’s
greed uses him as a tool to turn Australia into
a cheap, black man’s country, instead of a home
for decently-living]white men.
♦-
How New South Wales Government helps the
people to settle on the land ! A little while
ago, the lease of 60,000 acres of good agricul-
tural land, at Yalgogrin, was offered by
auction. In selling such an enormous mass of
territory in one lump all the small agricul-
turalists, and nine-tenths of the large ones,
were practically shut out. Asa natural con-
sequence only four buyers turned up, and
ultimately Robert Barbour, M.L.A., got a
20 years lease at £64 a year —about four
acres for one penny per annum. Now, it
would appear, the same Mr. Barbour is
offering the land in 640-acre lots at almost
100 times what he pays for it himself.
If this little scheme succeeds, therefore,
the State gets less than per cent, of the
rent paid by the agriculturist, and Barbour,
the middleman, get per cent. And it
almost certainly will succeed, for Barbour,
M.L.A., is a man who makes few mis-
takes in this class of business, and his
name is dotted all over the map of many a
parish, with the persistency of the inveterate
land-jobber. Therefore, what the country
wants to know is why the State couldn’t sub-
divide the land itself, instead of depending on
Mr. Barbour to act as middleman and scoop
98J per cent out of the profits?
Dr. Byrne, of Brisbane, the well-known
musical medico, bought a Bulletin at Melbourne
wharf the other day, and found that the boy had
foisted a two-year-old copy upon him. Somebody
will presently bo getting into trouble over this
impudent swindle.
Parkes has been getting (and, of course,
publishing in the dailies) another letter from
Hingland. It is from his ancient friend, G. J.
Holyoake, with whom he regularly swaps grease,
and who speaks of Parkes’ late volumes, “Fifty
Years of Australian History,” in these words :
“ They form a monumental record, amazing in
their variety and interest. They endow Australia
with new fortune in the eye of mankind.”
Tremendous ! The saliva-choppings of these
hoary egotists make the world smile.
Harold Stephen, novelist, journalist, play-
right and politician, himself always impecunious,
often lectured his father Milner, when the latter,
fallen also into impecuniosity, would persist in
living by his red flannel. “Go back to the Bar;
go back to some official position,” Harold would
say. “ There are 20 positions within your reach,
and all you have to do is to put out your hand for
the emoluments.” “My dear boy,” the father
would say, tenderly nursing a square of flannel,
“you know nothing about a vocation, a divine
mission. Lend me five shillings.”
A William Pearce Richards writer to the
Argus, explaining that “ certain injurious phases
of democracy have conspired to increase indiffer-
ence as to correct pronunciation and elegant dic-
tion.” The “Vicar of Bray” was a phase of
democracy. “ I have ceased tojspeak in rounded
periods,” said the curate, “and am convinced
that a strictly grammatical existence is not worth
living.”
What shocked Sydney Daily Telegraph
most about the scene in Assembly was
evidently the fact that Minister Copeland, daring
his fit of hyperaesthesia, wore a white coat and no
vest: —
The scene was an extremily painful one, Here was a
Minister of the Crown, dressed in a short white coat,
without a vest, hatless, and with his collar and tie in
disoider.
And again :
Mr. Copeland was rather peculiarly dressed for the
Assembly, appearing in a short duck coat, and without a
vest.
There ia something that is evidently in-
tended for pathos in the cable story of the last
moments of Captain Wilson’s contingent—the
famous 40 men who were wiped out in a desperate
attempt to capture the fallen Lobengula, “When
the Matabele made their final rush upon the
survivors of the party, they were found to be
alternately using their revolvers and writing
letters of farewell to their relatives.” The man
who writes farewell letters to his relatives
with one hand while he shoots with the other,
and does it amid the dust and frenzy of
a battle fought at close quarters, looks interesting,
no doubt, but he is just as impossible as the
slogger who writes to his sick child with one huge
fist while he bangs his six-foot antagonist with
the other, and yet doesn’t spill the ink in the
melee. This sort of muddy pathos, however,
helps to fill J. Bull with a vague sense of injury,
and a yearning for revenge upon the “savages”
who slaughtered his brethren in the midst of their
literary exertions. The bald fact that the man
who charges a retreating enemy with an unfinished
letter to his aged mother in his hand has to take
his chance like any other man, gets entirely over-
looked in the excitement.
Premier Patterson’s Prayer.
(Written for him after his address at the Presbyterian
Church, South Melboui ne.)
’Tis true the wealthy richer grow,
Whilst poor are waxing “ strfler ;
Their interests on earth below
Invariably differ.
But teach us. Lord, to keep the peace,
Likewise to love our neighbour ;
Let Fat man wallow in his grease,
And I will butter Labour.
The worker lean has work to sell;
Kind Capital can buy it.
Thou seest, Lord, that all is well,
Though Democrats deny it.
If Labour takes the lowest price,
Then virtue’s its rewarder.
Thy servant Patterson’s advice
I s _“ Stick to Lawan’order.”
We know our duty is to love,
And cheerfully to sutler ;
The hand enveloped in a glove
Should grip the paw that’s tougher.
An aged hat will warm the head,
Although it be not nappy ;
And no man needs a springy bed
As long as he is happy.
Grant wisdom, Lord, to simple toil.
Make angry strikers better ;
Preserve the owners of the soil,
And purify the sweater.
I see in Wrong oppressing Right
The golden rule of Mammon ;
But darkness giveth place to light
Before the Lamp of Gammon.
Ponce de Leon.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
By a black wharf I stood lately,
When the night was at its noon ;
Keen, malicious stars were shining,
And a wicked, white-faced moon.
And I saw a stately vessel,
Built in fashion quaint and old ;
From her masthead, in the moonlight,
Hung a flag of faded gold.
Black with age her masts and spars were,
Black with age her ropes and rails ;
Like a ghost through cere-clothes gazing
Shone the white moon through her sails
Not a movement stirred the stillness,
Not a sound the silence broke,
Save alone the livid water
Lapping round her sides of oak.
Then to her unseen commander
Spake I, as to one I knew
“ Don Juan Ponc6 de L6on,
I have waited long for you.
“ Take me with you, I implore you!
Take me with you on your Quest
For the Fount of Youth Eternal,
For the Islands of the Blest,”
Then, above the bulwarks black, I
Saw a spectral head arise ;
And the moon with ghastly glimmer
Lit its sad and hollow eyes ;
And a voice came from it, speaking
Low, in tones the heart to reach.
Very sweet and melancholy—
The grand old Castilian speech.
“ Grieved am I, senor, and sorry
Greatly,” were the words it said,
“ That I may not take you with me—
But I only take the Dead.
“ These alone may dare the voyage,
These alone sail on the quest.
For the Fount of Youth Eternal,
For the Islands of the Blest.”
VICTOR J. DALEY.
A Vilanelle.
(FOR THE BULLETIN.)
This is the need of the world as it swings
(Never a day was there need of it more) —
Songs that will teach us the falsehood of things:
Singers to thunder at thronings of kings,
“ What of the hungry and what of the sore?”
This is the need of the world as it swings :
Thunder as thunders the surf as it rings
Down on the dip of a desolate shore,
Songs that will teach us the falsehood of things:
Flashings of thoughts that have truth for their
wings,
Voicings of doubts that were voiceless
before—
This is the need of the world as it swings.
This is the need of the world as it flings
Forth from the clouds that engulfed it of
yore—
Songs that will teach us the falsehood of things.
Songs that will cut, through the glamour that
clings,
Into the truth of the innermost core ;
This is the need of the world as it swings.
Queensland.

“ Beauty and the Beast,” at Sydney
Lyceum, entered on its final fortnight last Satur-
day, but time has not tarnished its glittering glare*
—neither have the chorus-girls run short of paint.
If the rouge began to be precious at the end of the*
panto., or if the coal-dust that does duty for eye-
brows and lashes among the figurantes has to be*
toned down to 8.8. peneillings owing to the
supply giving out, the last stage of beauty might
be pleasanter than the first. But, as it is, the*
production is dying apparently of scenic apoplexy
with strawberry cheeks and undimmed draperies,,
and the person who hoped that time would tone*
down the chromotrope is doomed to disappoint-
ment.
• • •
A bright spot in the final fortnight is the
latest London craze, “ Linger Longer Lucy,” sung
by Nellie Stewart, with Florence Young lending
a voice here and there. “ Linger Longer Loo ” is.
a catchy chorus tied on to trashy verses, detailing
the well-worn fact that love laughs at locksmiths,,
of which you scarcely pick up the air ; but the
refrain, though idiotic, is alliteratively attractive,,
and ought to be a street nuisance in no time. As
a sentence for suspicious woman to store up in her
brain and make hubby pronounce it on lodge-
n ghts, “Linger Longer Loo” is unrivalled.
Neither Miss Nellie Stewart nor Miss Florence
Young had got the words properly under her
tongue on Saturday night, but a treble encore
followed the song and dance, which is propping
the panto, in its sere and yellow leaf. At the end
of “ Beauty,” Nellie Stewart takes a spell in the
Blue Mountains, previous to opening at Melb.
Princess’ in “Dorothy.”
** » *
The show at Sydney Opera House leads a
blameless existence as before in the presence of a
medium-sized audience. It is evident by this
time that the co. is not going to make any striking
success, the curse of the establishment being
against it; but at the same time it is not going to
fall with quite so hollow a thud as most of its pre-
decessors. This week’s programme consists of the
“Bohemian Girl,” an honourable, middle-aged
opera which has never done anything to awake
the suspicions of the police. The co. tackles this
production in a cordial spirit, and makes the old,
old story of the Czech maiden, who was stolen
from her father’s baronial pile, seem as new as
possible. Miss Colborne-Baber’s Arline takes
most of the honours ; she is in good voice, and the
ancient melody, “ I dreamt that I dwelt in marble
halls,” sounds better than it has done for several
years past. Edward Farley, whose Devilshoof
used to be a man of might, now plays the Count a
trifle stiffly, though he sings him with all his old
brilliance. As for Devilshoof, he has fallen into
the hands of a depressing person who need not be
farther mentioned. Jules Simonsen has given up
acting altogether, but he stands in a sternly verti-
cil attitude like a man who has had an unplea-
smtneSs with his joints, and sings “The Fair
Land of Poland ” for all it is worth, and with
much enthusiasm upon the top notes. That over-
rated song, by the way, isn’t, and never was,
worth very much. The orchestra, fortunately,
isn’t so vertical as Mr. Simonsen, and The
Bulletin hereby gives that orchestra its blessing,
as one of the best of its size this paper has met
with since the last time that Parkes conducted him-
self like a perfect gentleman. In fact, if it weren’t
for Mr. Jules Simonsen, to whom we recur with
reluctance, the show would be not so very far
from perfection. And if that gentleman would
infuse into Thaddeus a little more of the quality
that a man gets up off a hot stove with, it would
be a lot nearer perfection than it is. The “Grand
Duchess ” follows.
*m * »
The farther recurrence of “ Charley’s
Aunt,” at Sydney Criterion, steadily increases
The Bulletin’s wonderment at the utter common-
place of Thornton and Arnold’s carefully im-
ported actors. Thanks to the brilliance of Frank
Thornton, who infuses fresh blasts of humour into
the Brazilian widow almost every night, these
persons don’t seriously injure the comedy,
but still, when one considers the dead level
of their mediocrity, it is awful to remember
that they came from the other end of the
world to be so utterly ordinary and to
present so unvarying a resemblance to any-
body else. The Bulletin has for some time
back restrained itself with difficulty from saying
something unkind about these persons, and has
concealed its surprise at their existence with con-
siderable difficulty. When it sees them a few
times more, however, it is just possible that it
will oast off all restraint and use actionable
language towards three of them, anyhow.

Her Majqsty’s pantomime, “Jack the
Giant Killer,” will flicker out next Friday,
and then the era of the large, robust drama in a
great number of acts will return once more. A
vast, hunky production called “True as Steel”
inaugurates tha new season, and George Rignold
once more takes the exact centre of the stage with
the limelight turned on him, and a fixed resolution
to clear his character from unjust suspicion.
** * *
“The Cannon’s Mouth,” an entirely new
devastation in four acts, will riot on the stage at
Sydney Royal next Friday, thei’efore Jagon will
strangle his victim on Thursday for the very last
time.
** * *
Xmas lingers yet in Melbourne show-
businesa, and the ghost walks at four theatres
with varying strides. The Royal panto, presents
few changes in its “second edition.” Maggie
Moore has a new coster song, which she interprets
in her best pathetic style, but seeing that the ditty
deals for the most part with a dead baby, it is,
perhaps, a little too topical. Such recent events
as the hanging of Mrs. Knorr, and the suicide of
the gentleman first appointed to do that fell deed,
are recalled to mind, and the vision of a prison
“bath full of blood” obtrudes itself upon the
imagination. Also, dim recollections of policemen
digging for babies with large, official shovels are
revived in all their original awfulness. So the house
grows very hushed when Maggie Moore wipes the
corner of her eye, and the encore is for the artist
rather than for her song of death. Fred Garnet, the
quaint little acrobat clown is an added feature at
the Royal, where spectacular effects are con-
ducted with regard to economy, and both ends are
glad to meet on a purely commercial basis.
** * &
Caicedo, the bounding wonder of the wire,
went through a form of nearly losing his balance,
the other night, since when public interest in the
little South American Spaniard has made another
spurt. The thin edge of jocularity has managed
to get into that cumbersome sports procession at
the Princess’s. Possibilities of harmless merri-
ment are suggested by a mariner’s telescope
(15ft. high) and a gigantic breechloader (with
revolvers for arms) who single themselves
out from the dull crowd and strike up a weird
flirtation. An animated fishing-float bobs around,
too, at the same time, and maybe one of the
cricket-pads, or horseshoes, or sets of pyramid-
balls in a triangle, contains a youthful panto-
mimist who would gladly be funny if put in the
right way. These remarks apply also to the dolls’
procession, A large, undraped doll, with a ghastly
head on it, begins to play the fool in an intelligent
manner. For the rest of the pantomime, it is
much the same as usual, and cash returns are on
the old bloated scale. A very agile and brisk
harlequinade contributes its fair quota to the
general prosperity.
** * *
“ True Metal ” meets all requirements
the Alexandra. They have got the mule unde r
better control, and the nightly rescue of the rag-
and-bone boy from a blazing cellar is carried out
with a wealth of fiery detail that delights the
gallery. The bones have lost none of their ancient
smell, and the desperate struggle for twopence
between a gin-soddened husband and his hungry
old ’ooman increases in violence every evening.
This is indeed a great drama of its class, although
its class is that of the penny narrative which
makes strong men shudder. “True Metal” is
likely to be superseded by another of Dan Barry’s
earthquakes on Saturday.
»* » *
At Melbourne Alhambra a new item i s
Oloriz, a rope climber and monkey-actor, said to
be identical with one Pongo, who appeared at the
Princess’s recently, His feats are chiefly notable
for taking place close to the roof in an atmosphere
that must be very trying, anyhow. Exhibition
concerns continue to move with the times. Next
Saturday, afternoon and evening, the musical
performance becomes merged in the A.N. A. Fete,
and on two moonlit nights of this week the Ex-
hibition company were to embark in the Hygeia,
and sing to floating audiences at half-a-crown per
head. The Cyoloramas are specially recommended
as retreats from outside weather. Also, the
Bijou Theatre is a cool, shady place, having been
To Let since Xmas.
** * *
Showman Frank Clark didn’t cut such a
very poor figure in that maintenance case, after
all said and done. His letter to the girl, which
the papers printed with such gusto, was a
very kindly one; also, it transpired that he paid
a respectable official visit to the patient, when he
insisted upon dandling the baby and “seemed very
pleased at the idea of being a father.” Let us hope
that Clark didn’t hurt the infant’s back, or any-
thing, in his fresh, young enthusiasm. Up to
then, one imagines, his experience of nursing had
been limited to rag babies in Irish farces. He
would find a difficulty in curbing his professional
inclination to start a chorus and beat time with
his first-born.

Miss Ada Crossley, Australia’s great native-born con-
tralto, departs for England by the Britannia on March 14.
Ere leaving she will give farewell concerts at Sydney,
Melbourne and Adelaide.
The expectation that the comedian and the ballet-girl
and the baby would come on, and do a “ turn,” led to a
crowded house on the night after the settlement of that
Melb. maintenance ease.
Frances Saville has joined the Carl Rosa Co., and has
made a great hit in Scotland. They fairly rave about her
in Glasgow, where she appeared as Marguerite in
“ Faust.” The Northern critics are moat enthusiastic
about her singing.
Much interest is still felt in London re Mr. Fenwick,
who lately married Sylvia Grey. Australians say he is
American, and the Yankees return the compliment.
Wherever he was “raised,” he is a splendid rider to
hounds. His horses, too, are excellent, and he is tall,
good-looking, and agreeable. Just now he rivals Albert
Chevalier as the most fertile topic of conversation.
S.A. Register musical critic notices a moral service
and song, “John Dean’s Wife,” written by a Port
Adelaide man, and states the musical portion has been in
the hands of two people “who have adapted the music
of Haydn, Mozart and Beethoven to the words of the
hymn.”
Dear Bulletin,— Have you noticed that whenever a
travelling dramatic co. is announced to appear in a
country town, the local amateurs, or some hungry church
party, get up a concert or tea-meeting, a night or two
before appearance of the pro.’s? Thus, in most N.S.W.
country towns the local yahoos receive immense applause
from a large audience for butchering “The Midship-
mite,” dla chaffcutter, while a perhaps talented profes-
sional co. has to face an almost empty and silent house
on the following night! Only the other evening this sort
of thing happened in Warren. The local “ howlers and
hellpreaohers ” are a shabby 10t.—6 xB.
The just-deceased Sir John Don Wauchope, a very rich
and influential Scotch laird, inherited his baronetcy and
property at the death of his relative, Sir William Don,
once well-known in Australia as an actor. He owned
large estates which were heavily encumbered, hut they
have now become enormously valuable to the advantage
of the present Don Wauchopes (pronounced Walk-ups.)
Melbournian : “ I dropped recently into a church meet-
ing, where they debated church and stage. A young
man said he was a church member, also a playgoer, and
thought ‘Turquoisette ’ very bad taste in art, but
thoroughly proper. Another said: ‘The managers only
employ women in the ballet because they are cheaper
than men.’ I asked myself, ‘ls this innocence or
impudence?”*
“ Lex” : A woman’s marriage voids all other contracts.
If Vi Varley married Joe Tapley, or anyone else, all her
contracts would be sponged out. This was the case when
L. L. Lewis married Rosa Dunn, under Barry Sullivan’s
management, and she left the boards for ever, without a
day’s notice. Same again when Marcus Clarke married
Marion Dunn. If Varley and Tapley married now, they
would be discounted 40 per cent.
Xmas pantomime on at Irving’s Lyceum, London.
First in 25 years.
Dr. Neild was at home, in Melb. Shakespeare Society,
before the ladies, with a discussion on Falstaff. “The
poor fellow, you see, had drunkard’s heart. I assure you
I have out open hundreds of such.” The Shakesperiennes
turned qualmy.
“Oscar Barrett is going to give the Lyceum bars a
chance by making a 15 minutes’ interval in his play.”
The pantomime interval is quite new to London. Out
here they have been giving the bars two chances for years.
The Yankees have enthused over Tennyson’s “ Becket,”
and the present Irving-Terry tour is said to be beating all
previous records.
Pettitt, the dramatist, must have died rather suddenly,
for the Referee speaks of him “doing himself well” at
the Adelaide Gallery restaurant not more than _ two
months ago. His name crops up in connection with a
story of his old struggling days. Pettitt, it seems, once
offered a five-act melodrama to the Hebrew proprietors of
a very East-end London theatre. It was loaded up with
bigamy and gore, and he wanted £6 for it. Ultimately,
the Hebrews beat him down to “a ‘quid’ an act,” or £5
in all, and told him to think himself lucky. In Sept, last
these cormorants of auld lang syne came cringing to
Pettitt, with his horrible play in a black bag, and offered
to let him have it back for £5OO nett, or on terms._ They
gave him the option of re-writing it for their joint and
several benefit. Pettitt told the two gibbering Shylocks
to get out, whereupon they screamed through the key-
hole a ghastly threat to produce the £5 play in all its
early awfulneis, and blast his respectable name as a play-
wright. Poor Pettitt! It would make no odds to him,
now.
Miss Fortesoue continues touring the English provinces,
where she is a favourite, except at Newcastle-on-Tyne,
where the present Lord Cairns lived what time he was
simple Mr., and an of Armstrong, the big gun-
maker, and somewhat unchivalrously managed to imbue
the “ best people ” with a prejudice against her. The
Fortesoue holds herself quite aloof from her co., and
never speaks to any of them off the stage. Her leading
man, Vanderfelt, is madly in love with her, and shows it,
too, as Romeo, but she ll none of him. Indeed, she told
a lady, with whom she was staying recently, that
she didn’t suppose she would ever marry, as it is difficult
to find her ideal man. “He must lie handsome as a god,
amiable as an angel, virtuous, devoted, and rich as
Croesus.” When she finds such a one she will try and
forget “Gumboil,” who, of course, possessed all these
qualifications. Report says that Fortescue herself is far
from being an angel in temper.

guineas a week ; John Kemble, £36; whilst Edwin, the
best buffo and burietta singer that ever trod the stage,
received £l4 a week only.

“ I never go to the theatre, and if I have my way I
shall never set foot in it again, either in front or behind
the scenes.”—Mary Anderson.
Edwin Booth died worth £20,000, not counting £40,000
he gave the Players’ Club ere he died. Altogether he
made about £200,000 in 16 years.
Gov. William Wobinson’s new opera, played with
“immense success ” by amateurs before a Govt. House
audience at Perth, is called “The Handsome Ransom or
the Brigand’s Bride.”
Advt. of a music-hall song composer, from London
L’Entr’Actb: —“The Star Maker can always be found in
his office during office hours. His stock is too large to
carry in his pocket, and he does not care to hawk without
a licence. He has no time to loaf about street-corners,
but he will always be pleased to receive visits from
artists in search of good songs. He will not look vexed
if he has nothing suitable, but will he pleased to try
again. No attempt will be made to induce artists to buy
songs they don’t want, as McG. has his usual meals even
on days when he doesn’t sell a song. N.B.—Watch the
career of M'Glennon’s new author. His ideas are refresh-
ingly original. The Star Maker’s experienced hand will
guide him to a leading position in a short time.”
Alas, Poor Tinsley! We knew him well—a fellow of
infinite jest, most excellent humour—and to die of con-
sumption at 261
“ The smiles that men have made live after them,
The tears are oft interred with their bones—”
So let it be with Arthur Tinsley.
John R. Rogers (formerly Mr. Minnie Palmer) announces
that Minnie—who used to earn £5O a week—has now so
successfully disencumbered herself of her prestige that
she offered to play at London Shaftesbury without any
salary, as the only hope of getting a show. The oily little
John doesn’t seem to be growing any pleasanter as he
grows older.
Enriohetta D’Argo’s Spanish Dance is utterly lost in
Melbourne Princess’s Theatre Pantomime. The garish
chorus, in varying degrees of fatness and slimness, are
bunched all about her on the stage, and the gauze skirts
and pink fleshings look glaringly different and “ out of
the picture.” In a ballet-dance the “setting” some-
times is everything. So D’Argo, who dancers remark-
ably well, goes lor very little, while Mary Weir
and her sylphs, with “limes,” &c., get four encores
nightly. And if you want to see jealousy watch the
chorus “ darlings ” close in on D’Argo when she finishes
on tiptoe, and “ burke ” the applause.
Gallery boys at Melb. Alexandra don’t watch Barry’s
London mixture of vice and virtue; they watch Cope in
the orchestra blowing out candles. The Haleck doesn’t
sport electric-light at the conductor’s elbow, and “props”
gets candles for six, or something like it. When lights
go up, and villainy is unmasked, Cope puffs out his
farthing dip, and then in the most solemn passages a
copious spiral of yellowish smoke twirls upward
betwixt the audience and the stage, and people in the
d.c. have been heard to remark that it’s a shame to allow
the orchestra to smoke during the performance. The
" gods ” look for this now and never fail to emphasise.
A certain popular comedian was, a few years ago
directing a rehearsal, in Melbourne. Two painters’
suspended on a “tray” from the roof, were paint-
ing the proscenium. Presently, in the midst of the
rehearsing, a rope parted, and one of the men struck the
edge of the stage and rebounded off into the stall space,
where he lay huddled up. Everybody stopped short,
but—“ What are you wailing for? Go on," says the
comedian, and the rehearsal proceeded. The all-but
corpse was carried to the hospital, whence it did not
emerge for six months.
Nice, cheerful Christmas Joke in Garnet Welch’s
“book ” for Melb. Theatre Royal Pantomime. “ What do
you think will be the end of the human race ? ” “If
cremation has its way it will be a dead heat." The
audience take it like one unanimous mummy.
The Wild Cat Column.
Considering the wailings of English financial
authorities about the weakness of Australian banks, it
is interesting to note that the reserves of the big English
banks are all lamentably feeble, and far below even the
Australian standard. The huge National Provincial,
with 42 millions of deposits, has a cash reserve of 12 per
cent. The London and County (deposits, 33 millions)
has 11J per cent. ; Lloyd’s (24 millions deposits) hrs llj
percent., and the Capital and Counties (with nearly 12
millions of deposits) has only 11 per cent, cash reserve.
The rest of the big banks average about 14 per cent.,
while many of the Australian banks go as high as 30 per
cent. But for the fact that they have the Bank of Eng-
land to support them in times of stress, and that the
Bank of England has the State to fall back upon, the
English banks would, before this, have made the grandest
financial smash on record.
* # #
The liquidator of the Federal Bank has at last
decided—l 2 months after the falling-in of the late
lamented institution—to make a call of £1 per share. All
this time the creditors of the Federal have been waiting
patiently for information about its prospects, or for a
small dividend, or anything, but so far they have waited
mostly in vain.
* * #
The balance-sheet of the City Bank of Sydney,
for the half-year ending 31st December, 1893, furnishes
some more depressing literature for shareholders. The
net profits for the half-year are £10,735 19s. Bd., and a
little dividend at the rate of 6 per cent, per annum
absorbs £10,418 Is. 4d., so that the bank is £317 18s. 4d.
stronger at the end of the half year than it was at the
beginning. The result is too melancholy to be called
progress or anything else of a definite character, still it
is a trifle better than that shown in last balance-sheet
when, after paying the same obscure dividend, the bank’s
position was “strengthened” by carrying forward
£8 4s. Bd. It seems a pity that the City doesn’t reduce
its dividend to 5 per cent, at the very outside, and make
at least a small addition to- its reserve. In times
like these, the bank that can only add £426 3s. to its
funds as the result of a year’s hard work is a very potter-
ing little institution. The City Bank, according to its
balance-sheet, has now a paid-up capital of £358,786, and
a reserve of £219,393, making in all £578,179. Even if it
can be supposed that there is no profit whatever on the
note circulation and the investment of deposits, the in-
terest earned on the shareholders’ funds for the last 12
months has been less than four per cent. This is a
tolerably desolate result, and if the City put its capital
and reserves into N.S.W. Government stocks, and gave up
the perilous banking business altogether, it would be
better for the shareholders by at least a fraction per cent.
The note-issue of the City Bank, which, before
the time of the legal tender proclamation, was under
£34,000, is now £126,830. During the garish period when
lOU’s were legal tender the issue rose to £176,217, and
the bank whoops considerably over the reduction. But
even now the City Bank circulates very nearly twice
as many notes as the Union, which does a vastly
larger business. The scheme of issuing £1,000,000 worth
of new shares is still being pushed forward, though the
question of how the public is to be induced to take up
new shares at par, when old ones are to be bought
in the open market at a heavy discount, remains
as unfathomable as ever. And, even apart from
this consideration, the lamentable £426 35., which remains
over and above the year’s dividend, is not a specially
brilliant inducement to any capitalist.
* • #
The reconstruction scheme of the N.Z. Loan
and Mercantile Agency Co. is a lamentably confused
arrangement, but considering the awful manner in which
the co. liad tied itself up among ordinary debenture-
holders, and preference debenture-holders, and extra-
preference debenture-holders, it could hardly be other-
wise. The new oo.’s first liability, if the scheme is
adopted, will be the “prior lien debenlurestock "bearing
4 per cent, interest and secured by a first mortgage on all
the uncalled-capital, a specific first mortgage on the oo.’s
gilt-edged securities, and a floating lien upon everything
it possesses down to the gum-brush. Also, when this
stock is redeemed it is to be done at 2J per cent, pre-
mium. Then follows the “A ” debenture stock at 5 per
cent interest, which is to be secured by a second mort-
gage on the uncalled capital and all the co.’s possessions,
assets, hopes, and everything else. This lot is to be re-
deemed at 5 per cent. premium. After that comes the
“ B” debenture stock, at 5 per cent, interest, with a
third mortgage over every thing, and also to be redeemed at
5 per cent, premium. The premium seems to be a humble
and unobtrusive grab which is unavoidable under the
circumstances, the old extra-preference debenture-holders
being bosses of the situation. After this the ordinary,
everyday creditors look in, and in case of a collapse
they will be duly paid after the prior lien debentures and
the “ A” debentures and the "B" debentures have all
been satisfied. Next come the preference shareholders,
with 160,000 shares, paid up to 70s. Then the first batch
of ordinary shareholders-40,000 £lO shares fully paid up.
And at the miserable tail of the procession comes the
second batch of ordinary shareholders, representing
280,000 £lO shares paid up to 30s.
The shareholders are to be fixed on a fearful
and wonderful plan. In the first place, 30s. a share is to
be written off, apparently as dead loss that somehow
didn’t appear in the duly-audited balance-sheet. This
amount has certainly not been lost by the stoppage of
business, for the business has been conducts d
as before, all through the suspension, under a
slightly - altered style, and has actually increased,
instead of diminished. Therefore, there is room
for much hard inquiry as to why the old balance-sheet
concealed this enormous loss, or else as to why the new
balance-sheet shows a losst.that doesn’t exist. After this
writing off, the holders of 140,000 shares written down to
£1 per share are required to take up 280,000 shares
credited to 10s. each, and to pay up £1 on each of these
shares, making the paid-up capital 30s. per share; and
further, to take 140,000 preference shares, and pav on
them £3 10s. per share. The total cabs will be equal to
£5 10s. on the shares in the old company. Then the
holders of the 20,000 fully paid-up shares, written down
to £23 Ios., will receive 40,000 shares fully paid-up to £lO,
and 20,000 preference shares paid to £3 10s,
When all this confusion is accomplished, the
big Loan and Mercantile Co.—with three mortgages on its
uncalled capital and three separate liens over everything
it possesses—will be in a melancholy state. A concern so
hideously hampered, and unable to take any decisive
steps in a crisis without the consent of three separate sets
of shareholders and three conflicting gangs of debenture-
holders, is bound to come to grief some time unless it gets
rid of some of i(s burdens. The best—if not the only pros-
pect—is to abjure dividends for ten years or so, until the
prior lien debenture stock, and the “A” debenture stock,
and that other incubus, the “ B” debenture stock, is paid
off, with the solemn curse and objurgation of all the pre-
ference shareholders, and the ordinary shareholders fully
paid-up, and the ordinary shareholders paid up to 305.,
and everybody else concerned. And, in the meantime,
the shareholders would do well to inquire about the
sudden vanishment of that £240,000 out of the assets, and
find out why ihe last balance-sheet said that things were
all right, when it now appears they were so very far wrong.

The bloated reconstruction scheme of the Com-
mercial Bank of Australia seems coming home to roost.
Last half-year the newly phcenixed bank paid no div. and
carried forward £26,141. The net profits for the present
half-year amount to rather less than £30,000, and as a
five per cent. div. on preference shares alone absorbs
£53,750 it follows that it has taken practically a whole
year’s profits to pay a half-year’s div. on preference shares
only. The telegraphic announcement of this weepful
circumstance winds up with the statement that, “ordinary
shares do not, therefore, participate this half-year.”
“This half-year ” is good, tor if business continues at its
present rate, the ordinary stock won’t participate either
next or any other half-year as long us the bank lasts, and
the preference shareholders will have to be content here-

The Bank of Victoria pays a dividend at the
rate of five per cent, per annum on preference and
ordinary shares alike, and carries forward nearly £5OOO.
The result is not brilliant in itself, but it seems sound,
and cjmpared to the results obtained by the Commercial
of Australia it is Oriental magnificence.
# # *
The Victoria Insurance Co. is about the steadiest
and most absolutely reliable institution .of its kind in
Australia. For 1893 it has paid about 15 per cent in divi-
dends, established a contingency fund of £15,000, and
has £12,000 left to carry forward.
» * •
The reconstructed bank of North Queensland
still tackles its difficulties in a vigorous spirit. After pay-
off the remainder of its reconstruction expenses it
carries forward its whole available balance of about
£5OOO. Tins bank has shut down with a strong hand on
share-transfers till it is again in a dividend-paying state,
so that in case of further trouble the rich shareholders
can’t pass their shares on to men of straw and stand from
under themselves
* # *
The A.J.S. Bank shows a net profit for the
half-year of £51,000, out of which it pays a 5 per cent,
dividend (about £23,000); places a similar amount to the
reserve fund, which now stands at £35,000 ; and carries
the balance forward. A bank which earns 11 per cent,
a id only pays away five in dividends is on the right road.
The A.J.S. balance-sheet is much more comprehensible
than the average, it has a large cash reserve, and its
prospects seem generally good. Much of its loose cash
seems to be held in British consols.

# • •
A certain provincial N.S.W. Building Society
has deemed it necessary to send for a prominent account-
ant to overhaul its affairs. The local auditors signed the
balance-sheet, but repented thereof, and begged the secre-
tary to withdraw their signatures prior to the holding of
the convened meeting. The secretary declined and pre-
sented the balance-sheet in due coarse. Hence the
trouble.
