• • •
A certain coal-mine proprietor lately returned
from a trip to Europe. The day after his arrival he pro-
ceeded to his office and gave notice of dismissal to all
hands, from the manager to the miners. Then he offered
them their respective billets back at reduced salaries,
with the simple explanation that wages were less in
Europe. There is evidently a bad time ahead for his em-
ployes should that mine-owner ever visit a mine in, say,
Japan.
tt • •
Apropos a big (impending) failure for which the
wrong man is “carrying the baby.” A partner in the
firm who “knew a bit” invested, 18 months ago, some
£12,000 of the firm’s money in a mine (a private affair)
of which, we will say, Mr. Jobson was manager. Jobson
levanted ; the mine turned out a dead swindle, and the
sequel is embedded in the following par from a South
Country paper of Oot. last: “The Primitive Methodist
connection of this town are invited for Wednesday next
to attend the house-warming of Mr. Jobson who, four
years ago, was the most zealous and active of the local
preachers in the connection. It Will be remembered that
the good gentleman left the town some four years since to
try his fortune at the Antipodes. Qod has blessed him.
He discovered a mine from which he has reaped over
£lO,OOO, and he has returned to his native town, pur-
chased land, built himself a house, and will shortly take
to himself a wife to share with him the fruits of his enter-
prise in Sunny Australia.” If that partner could only
see that paragraph, what price that happy home 1 And
isn’t it cruel for a busted speculator to be termed “ a
mine” by a local preacher?

Another version of it“ When he discovered
the Reward mine and showed it to one or two of his
friends, they asked, ‘ Why don’t you break into such a
rich face of reef and see how far it goes?’ ‘No,’said
Bayley, ‘ I want to sell the mine. I’ve spoilt more than
one goo,l-looking thing through over-anxiety.’ Along
came the syndicate ; they saw the promising face and
made an offer of £6OOO down and a-sixth share in the
mine. ‘Right,’ said Bayley, ‘it’s a bargain.’ And so it
was. And that is about the only sane theory on which
one can account for Bayley’s sale to Browne. Despite
the magnitude of Browne’s “punch,” Bayley did what in
90 cases out of 100 is emphatically the right thing. One
Bulletin proverb is: “ Lay to your book.” Another is:
Never punch a good face.”

Government recently sold with such a tremendous whoop

During 1894, Victorian shareholders in recon-
structed banks have to pay £1,800,000 in calls. Just
£200,000 over the value of that province’s wheat export 1
“ The harvest,” by the way, is always depended upon by
most Melb. distributors to reduce the volume of
“renewals” required 10 months out of 12 by country
storekeepers. It looks as if the banks will get the cash
and the merchants the paper this year, anyway.

Suppose an irreligious devil who was lucky
enough to have two banking accounts, were to induce
three friends (equally irreligious) banking at other banks
tp work accommodation paper for, say, £300,000. Sup-
pose that none of the “paper” was of any value, how
much “time” would that poor devil of a financier get
when found out? One right-thinking Australian is
credited with having thus worked seven banks.
* * #
Manager Begelhole, of Bayley’s Reward,
formerly managed the Great Northern at Drake, N.S.W.,
abandoned as a waster, but now a substantially good
property. A Bulletin correspondent from that locality
jibes at the Begelhole’s sensational report on Bayley’s,
lately printed in this paper. This manager, says his
critic, seems to be adopting at Coolgardie his practice of
gutting a mine for rich dabs. Perhaps so, but it is im-
possible to under-estimate Drake mines. Most of them
are incapable of too much condemnation. After all, it is
human for mining managers to over-estimate poor mines
and to under-estimate very rich ones. Bayley’s is un-
doubtedly one of the latter, and if Begelhole is an excep-
tion to the rule he is a fool to his own reputation.
# • #
They don’t back miners at Coolgardie and
Murchison. A Murchison storekeeper advertises:—“A
fortnight’s credit given to wage-men but all others must
pay cash.”
# * •
Miller, Vic. railway contractor—did he own the
Union mine in the Northern Territory ?—advises the
erection of crushing machinery on the banks of Swan
River (VV.A.) for the treatment of Coolgardie and Southern
Cross ores to be conveyed there by rail. The idea is
approved of. In the flat country of these fields a narrow-
gauge line costs less than £2OOO a mile, and ore could be
profitably carried at less than a penny a mile. If the
Coolgardie big reefs will go loz. to the ton, here is a solu-
tion of the water difficulty. Low-grade ores are carried
200 miles in the States for treatment.

» * *
Premier Dibbs says the N.S.W. M. on P. P.
Bill will give work to 15,000 men. Sheer humbug. Four-
fifths of N.S.W. is already open to mining, and the
owners of fully four-fifths of the balance would be agree-
able to throw open their lauds if prospects warranted it.
An Act is needed, and must be passed, but it will probably
not find remunerative work for 1500 men.
• # *
Vic. ’93 gold-yield, .£2,700,000, or about ,£ll2 a
man for the 24,000 miners employed. Nothing to boast
of, but nearly £2O a man more than 10 years ago. N.S.W.
figures not available, but compare those of Bathurst
mining district which includes many well-known fields :
output, £38,000, or about £25 per man for about 1500
employed.

• • #
Last year the big Broken Hill mine broke the
world’s record by producing 12J million ozs. of silver and
nearly 50,000 tons lead. Value of metals, nearly three
millions—more than that of all the gold produced last
year by Queensland or Victoria.
Mitchell’s Creek Freehold, N.S.W., reports
treating nearly 3000 tons tailings by cyanide—averaging
Bto lOdwts. It is from a heap of some 30,000 tons left
many years ago. They say nothing about the value of
the gold thus discovered. The writer happened to see
some bars of it a few days ago, and at once concluded
that it was not worth much over £2 10s. an oz.
* # *
Peak Hill Proprietary’s 35-head battery still
shut down for want of water. It should teach the manage-
ment a lesson.
* # *
Next to falling off a log the easiest thing is to
run a mining-share barometer of dividend-paying mines.
You, of course, predict a rise when a div. is approaching,
and a fall after it is paid ; then, the following week,
allusion to the div., you show your own
accuracy to the unthinking.

Two months ago, twenty Queenslanders —miners
all—left for South Africa. Writing from the Cape they
spin a doleful yarn about its overcrowded state, and they
hear there the same story of the Kandt, where 500
arrive every week from England alone.
Mt. Allen is another N.S.W. mine whose share-
holders are bursting with indignation. Good body of oz.
ore, fine 20-head mill, but no water ; worse still, precious
little provision made to conserve any. It is time snare-

A dog-cart, with “Debt Collector” painted
around it, and drawn by a dun pony, is now an
everyday sight in some parts of Sydney. The man who
wore the w r hite paper hat emblazoned with a similar
legend and who took his place on the steps of the debtor’s
hall-door at 10 a.m. and did not retire till nightfall, came
to legal grief many years ago, owing to the decision of a
Sydney Justice, and it may, therefore, come to pass that
the mission of the dog-cart and dun pony will be a
troubled one.

The output of sugar for Queensland for 1893
was nearly 81,000 tons, valued at £1,000,000. For the
current year 100,000 tons are expected. The Sugar Works
Guarantee Act should, in 1895, bring the production up
to 140,000 tons.

* * *
Judged by results to date, Murchison (W.A.)
goldfield is far better for working miners than Coolgardie.
Gf nine Victorian expeditions to Coolgardie, eight have
returned dead-broke, and the other (five members) drank
itself to death ; whereas, of ten expeditions to Mur-
chison, eight are doing exceedingly well. The treasurer
of one was lambed-down by a siren at Geraldton, and the
other expedition is distributed promiscuously around the
hotels of Freemantle and Perth.
• « *
The Victorian railway department has tried the
apparently reckless experiment of reducing fares on the
unprofitable Whittlesea line by CO per cent. The Whittle-
sea line was selected because it was already so bad that a
change could hardly, by any possibility, be for the worse.
The results, within a fortnight, were that the traffic in-
creased fourfold and the takings almost doubled. Next
time Victoria or N.S. Wales has a decent surplus it might
be almost worth while to reduce all fares 50 per cent, and
darn the consequences. It is just possible that the result
would be such a period of bloated affluence as no Aus-
tralian railway department has yet witnessed.
# # #
During the past year, United States railway
companies, with 18,000 miles of railways, have gone bank-
rupt, liabilities £162,600,000. The U.S. railway com-
panies, taken as a whole, are, from a financial standpoint,
about the worst-managed on earth, and those people
who are always clamouring for a U.S. “ expert ” to hurry
the Australian railway systems into general bankruptcy
would do well to make a note of it.
* # •
It is about time that something of a violent
character were being done in the matter of the great Melb.
Premier Permanent Building Society. For years warrior
Templeton has been drawing a huge salary as liquidator.
The big case against the directors is ancient history.
James Mirams’ umbrella was tried and sentenced
to 18 months gaol, and the expiration of the sentence is a
very old story now. And yet the Premier has paid no
div. to its creditors, and there is no sign of a dividend. The
liquidator who is paid a salary has no interest in hurry-
ing, and the liquidator who is paid by a percentage on
receipts makes a £lO,OOO call to make up a £lOO shortage,
and then collects his commission on the £lO,OOO and
hands back the balance. Either way the creditors get
left.

Whales are being captured in nets on the
M.L. coast. An observant whaler, having noticed
for several seasons that the whales, in their jour-
neys southward, invariably passed between a
small island and the mainland, blocked the little
strait with nets, and then beset the nonplussed
animals with harpoon and lance. As many as six
whales were thus caught in one day last season.

Editor Bulletin, —The swagman isn’t
really the hushworker’s worst enemy, as
“ Waipoie ” asserts. The bushworker’s tenure of
his billet is menaced chiefly by the shiftless “ cock-
atoo,” and the bell-bottomed hobbledehoy from
town and city, which two (species of “scab,”
always willing to work more cheaply than anyone
else, Chinamen not excepted, have now got
possession of the bulk of the bush work in N.S.W.
The average swagman isn’t much pumpkins, but
where the rate of wages is concerned, as a rule he
doesn’t “creep ” while he can buy, beg, or steal a
bit of food. —Whalebone.
The son of an English rector has just been
bowled out in the meanest fraud of the century.
He used to advertise in the London papers for a
governess to go to Maoriland and take charge of
his children, and the successful applicant was
always urged to lay in a good stock of clothes
as such tilings were very dear in “ the kawlinies.”
Then he professed to secure the girl’s passage by
steamer, and used to take her to Fenchurch-street
station where he left her in the waiting-room
while he got her boxes on board. At this stage
he always fled with the boxes, and having sold the
clothes would have an orgie upon the proceeds.
It takes a tolerably hardened parson’s son to run
a swindle of this sort.
London Referee is rough upon a hreach-
of-promise case jury:—“A brewer’s traveller,
earning two or three pounds a week, makes the
acquaintance of a dressmaker, whom he first sees
smoking a cigarette at an open window. They
walk out together for some weeks, in which the
plaintiff allows herself to be seduced in a public
lane. His promise to marry her is a verbal one.
By accident she subsequently discovers that he is
a married man, and the jury award her .£5OO.
What did the plaintiff really lose in this ease,
besides that honour which she gave away by the
roadside in the bitter month of November? A
prospective husband in receipt of two or three
pounds a week. On the same scale a man earning
,£3OOO a year would have been oast in .£lO,OOO
damages.” Perhaps the jury thought that the
affliction of a girl who loves not wisely under such
chilly circumstances was a matter for substantial
sympathy. Evidently she had given all her heart
to the brewer’s drummer.
Messrs. Hordern Bros., of Pibt-street,
Sydney, have just completed the first week of their
gigantic “All Nations Sale.” Bands have been
playing all day, the flags of all nations have
been flying, music of all nations, including the
skirl of the bagpipes, has been rendered, and
people of all nations have congregated round.
All the stock has been reduced to the lowest
reconstruction prices. In the carpet-window a
bedroom was arranged, and a boy was calmly
lying in bed to lend an air of reality to the sur-
roundings (the fact that the boy, in a moment of
excitement, turned in with his boots on is a mere
circumstance). Country people are notified, on
page 19, that their wants shall receive prompt
attention, and goods will be sent awav the Same
day the order is received.

“IMITATION
is the
SINCEREST FLATTERY.”
The articles most imitated are
CLEMENTS TONIC and
FLETCHER’S PILLS.
WHY?
BECAUSE they have the best reputation and
are most in demand by the public, consequently,
every dishonest trader tries to trade on their fame
and renown.
It is really wonderful that the Australian people
are so slow to see the truth as regards the un-
scrupulous quacks and charlatans who prey on the
credulity of the Australian public. Scarcely a
week ever passes but what the press exposes the
barefaced and shameful practices of some of these
harpies, yet, in face of all they seem to thrive,
and the paper who hounds them down most
thrives fattest on their advertisements and cracks
champagne over each balance-sheet. Clements
Tonic and Fletcher’s Pills have never yet stooped
to deception and misrepresentation. They are
scientific therapeutical agents, and evidence as to
their value can be found in every district, and
such being the case it is unnecssary to further
xtol their virtues.

In March the Czar completes the thirteenth
year of his reign—thirteen is the “devil’s
number.” If he gets through March safely he
may dodge Nihilism altogether.

his Lordship popular in Heaven !

body, a cool head and warm feet, a man re’igious
in his principles, and who'never pays cash without
reflection.
London papers of 9th Dec. take from a
quarter to half-a-column to say that Gladstone
ate something which disagreed with him, and
caused stomach-ache. Luckily, he was better
almost as soon as he was bad ; so the great heart
of the nation didn’t have to throb as much as
might reasonably have been expected.

One of Toby Barton’s stories about Reid :
A gentleman came from some distance to Sydney, and
asked the gentleman who was entertaining him, “ What
is the reason that Reid has not been a loader before now,
seeing that he is a gentleman of some ability?” The
answer was, “They did not look upon him as a serious
politician.” “And do they now?” was asked.
reply was, “They now think he is a serious mistake.”

Millionaire Sydney Burdekin said lately
that he will not again accept the Mayoralty of
Sydney until the salary of the post is raised from
£llOOO to 161500. Yet Burdekin could live well
on the interest of the interest of his interest.
Tommy Walker has, in N.S.W. Assembly,
denounced G. H. Reid for taking liberties with
the name of the Almighty. And yet, not so very
long ago, Tommy used to take up a collection at
the door for doing the very same thing himself.
During his recent visit to this country
Organist Best, of Liverpool (Eng.), attended an
organ-recital at Sydney University given by a
..well-known looal performer. The consequent
nervous excitement of that gentleman was quite
unnecessary, as the veteran was discovered to bo
fast asleep after the second item, and he remained
so to the end.
Australians will not be surprised to hear
that the Duke of Edinburgh did not surrender
his allowance of .£25,000 a year from the British
tax-payer until both Gladstone and Salisbury had
assured him that he would not bo allowed to
keep it.
W. H. Gibson, governor of Castlemaine
(Vic.), gaol, has witnessed and superintended
upwards of 100 executions. It was he who
instructed the late “Jones ”in his “ work.” He
was present at Mrs. Knorr’s execution to prevent
a botch.

Dr. Maloney, of West Melbourne (one of
the delegates to the parliamentary Labour Con-
ference at Sydney), has christened his son
and heir “Danton.” It is to be hoped he will
have a better fate than his godfather, who,

years ago.

To show his authority and nautical knowledge he

Queen Victoria grows old. Lifts replace
stairs at various royal residences and steep
gradients on various favourite royal paths are
being heavily sloped. Why isn’t Royalty im-
mortal? There is a feeling in J. B. Pattersonian
circles that the Almighty might really manage
these things better.
Madeline Smith, accused of doing to death
Emile L’Angelier, married a doctor, who took her
to West Australia. He, as usual in small places,
had a chemist’s shop, and people were puzzled out
of their wits, because they could never see the
lady who was tickling the piano in the back
parlour. Afterwards they went to Melbourne—
and separated.
George Milner Stephen, professional
“faith-healer,” barrister-at-law and erstwhile
Acting-Governor of South Australia, is dead,
aged 82. He was a brother of the N.S.W. Ex-
Chief Justice, who has passed 90 years. Every-
thing has its compensations. Alfred Stephen
helped to hang far more men than his brother
Milner ever helped to heal.
Hangman Jones : —“ That Deeming was a
straightout cow. He screamed all night. I goes
to him in the morning to pinion ’im, and ’ee says
to me, ‘Oh, Jones, ’ave mercy.’ ‘What can I
do?’says I. “If I didn’t ’ang you the Sheriff
will, and ’ee’s a gentleman and does not care for
them jobs.’ He took a bottle of brandy the morn-
ing he ’anged, and that didn’t nerve ’im.”

A fawning lady of the N.S.W. “ military
set,” who had long grovelled to the Dibbs party,
seeing Parkes’stock rising in the market, visited
the old man, whom she had long neglected. “ Ah,”
said she, “poor old General Richardson—l am
afraid he is done for.” “Yes,” said Parkes
sweetly, “and I suppose that during the years
you haven’t been to see me you have been saying
all the time: ‘ Poor old Parkes— he is done for !’ ”
Lady Gertrude Stock, daughter of the late
Marquis of Queensberry, is dead. She some years
ago married a lad who had been brought up in a
London home in which she was interested, and
who had become a baker. He now lives in South
Africa. Lady Gertrude was a sister of our recent
visitor, the present Lord Queensberry (who so
adored Marion Hood that lie travelled round the
world in the wake of the Gaiety company), and of
that beautiful romancer, Lady Florence Dixie.
Premier Kingston (S.A.) has a hideously
careless style of pronunciation. “ Bona fides,”
for instance, he pronounces as if it rhymed with
“ moany tides.” Arguing a case before Way,
C.J., lately, he kept on making use of the phrase.
Nesbit, the opposing counsel, happened to talk of
dolus modus, which is lawyer’s Latin for fraud.
“ Dolus malus!” interjected Kingston, “ I don’t
believe that my learned brother knows the mean-
ing of dolus mahis.” “ Oh, yes, I do, y’Onah,”
replied Nesbit, “I mean exactly the opposite of
what Mr. Kingston calls * Bony fides.’ ”
' Dr. Quick, whose portrait appears in this
issue, is a well-known Bendigo ( Vic.) lawyer, and
an enthusiast on the subject of Australian Federa-
tion. The doctor has just visited Sydney with the
draft of a Federal Constitution, to replace the
cumbrous device of the almost-forgotten Conven-
tion, and so far his scheme, to which The Bulle-
tin purposes making detailed reference, has been
well received by the public.
Deputy Smithers, who has a 34in. chest,
has been appointed to succeed Police Magistrate
O’Malley Clarke at Sydney. Mr. Smithers is all
very well, but he ought not to have been chosen.
He is not fat enough. No lean man should be put
on the Bench. All judicial officials should be fat.
There never was a lean Judge in Australia who
was a success. Lean men are querulous—devoid
of staying-power and patience, and hopelessly
addicted to longing for dinner-time. Justice will
never have a chance until all the lean Judges and
magistrates are killed and their places supplied by
fat men sworn to rise early every morning and
rush round the park in sweaters.
Dr. QUICK, LL.D.
A wicked N.Q. paper tells this about
Scotch Mathieson, who is Chief Railway Com-
missioner at >£3ooo a year and two guineas a day
travelling extras, but canny and frugal, ye ken.
Mathieson, on one of his inspection pleasure-
trips, camped at a certain Northern pub., and
strolled into the coffee-room. It was full of jovial
bagmen, and the magnate unbent and chatted
without thought of distinction or degree. By
and by the Commissioner felt dry ; so did the
commercials. The Commissioner touched the
bell and the waiter came. “Bring me a hore-
hound,” said the Commissioner. The drummers
stared ; Mathieson drank his horehound and left.
And now he is known up North as “Solo Hore-
hound.”
Mention in The Bulletin of Nov. 4
the death of “Teddy ” Wrench, who married, en
second noccs, the widow of poor Throckmorton
(whose remains lie in an unmarked grave by the
wayside on the road to the Palmer, N.Q.), reminds
a Bulletin London correspondent of an amusing
cricket-match in which old “ Throck ” took part.
In those halcyon days there was a large American
mercantile contingent in Melbourne, jolly fellows
who made money rapidly and spent it like water.
Amongst them one of the most prosperous was the
big-hearted Throckmorton. Cricket was then all the
rage in Victoria, so the good idea occurred to him
to organise a “muff, match” for the benefit of
Melb. Hospital. The day arrived, sides were
chosen, the captains tossed for choice of innings,
and “Throck.,” who was one of them, lost, and
his men had to take the field. But how was he to
place them? He knew nothing of cricket, and his
men were no wiser. Happy thought —consult
Sam Cosstick. No sooner said than done, and
burly Sam was presently seen to emerge from the
pavilion with a case of champagne on his shoul-
ders, and proceeded to place a bottle where each
man should stand. This done, “Throck.” gave
the word of command : “ Boys, man your bottles!”
which the “ boys ” at once did, and emptied them,
too, amidst great laughter. And that is how
“ Throck.” placed his field.

tions. Sheriff Oowper, of Sydney, finds that since
he attained such ill-repute as a jury-host, jurors
come more quickly to agreement.

is fond of visiting Pentridge “to study human

prisoners in cold blood—after all the numerous

“Abuse is not the sign of a vigorous
intellect,” says the profound Argus. Neither is
a tall hat, nor a rounded period, nor a classical
education. But a vigorous intellect is a sure sign
of abuse for the man who owns it.

There is one thing worse than “party”
government, viz., coalition government of the
kind transacted in Victoria during the boom
period. “Set a thief to catch a thief” is the
principle that partially apologises for the folly of
“ party ” legislation.
A N.S. Wales M.P., who does not propose
to contest under the new law, asks £5O for a
compilation made by him of the political opinions
of all the voters in his electorate. In most cases
the opinions are vouched for by private letters
from the qualified voters.

One of the first things enfranchised woman
should strive for is her fair share in law adminis-
tration. An alien, under trial, may claim that
half his jury shall be of his own nationality, and
there are more and greater reasons why a woman
under trial should have her sex represented in the
jury-box.

Quoth Tiger Inglis, at his recent “ send-
off ” : “God help the country whose merchants
are not prosperous ! ” Quite so !—only this is
saddling the wrong horse! The unprosperous
huckster is an effect of commercial debility—not
the cause thereof. It is for his toothache we pity
the man with the swollen jaw—not for the
sympathetic tumefaction.
It was pretty to see N.S.W. Minister
Copeland—before the “ scene” on Jan. 17 —walk-
ing hand in hand up the floor of the Assembly
with millionaire Burdekin. Both short and fat;
both arrayed in whitecoats; and both looking as
jolly as sandboys. But the great Burdekin went
home early. If Copeland had done the same,
things would be different.
There is in course of compilation a volume
which will show alphabetically the names of all
Australian MsL.A. who, after election to Par-
liament, became insolvent, the sums for which
they failed, and the amounts of their realised
assets. The inference the compiler proposes to
draw is that membership of the Legislature has
financially ruined more than it has made.
The Q. Labour-party leaders seem deter-
mined to have the whole hog or nothing. They
will probably get nothing. The number of Q.
Unionists is small, after all, as compared with the
mass of population ; and to preach extremist doc-
trines is the surest road to failure. The Q. Labour-
party is badly in need of brains and discretion—
at the top. Lower down, there is plenty.

The vexed question as to the superior
policy of raising or lowering fares and freights in
times of depression seems settled. You must
raise them where rich passengers, or people living
in places from which they are obliged to travel
and where no rival conveyance is provided, reside,
but lower them under all other conditions.
Yic. Public Works Department has taken
the initiative in State socialism. The village
settlers at Koo-wee-rup—a recently reclaimed
swamp—having complained of the exorbitant
prices charged for provisions by the local store-
keepers, the Department promptly established
a Govt, store, selling at only a shade over whole-
sale prices. At latest, the settlers were highly
pleased at the change. _ .
It is a good thing to be a British official in
a rising colony, before the colony has obtained
representative Government. Even if he stays but
a few weeks, he is sure of a snug pension on his
return. Take the case of Wharton, the lory Bill-
blocker. As a reward for party services, Salisbury
gave him the Westralian Attorney-Generalship.
He has now returned to England, and has a life-
pension of .£4OO per annum. _

Sydney newspapers are inundated with
letters, purporting to be from the unemployed, and
making extraordinary claims in their interests,
but in the majority of cases written by clerks in
importing warehouses. A brief examination of
these revolutionary missives lately showed that
they all were the work of two hands and that tne
owners of the hands never did one stroke of the
work for which the unemployed are calling.
The dull, pompous commonplaceness of
Duncan Gillies came out strong when Munro rose
to move the death-warrant of his exhausted
Ministry. The little, shiny, fat man folded his
arms upon his chest, and said, “ Long expected
come at last.” That was his carefully-prepared
notion of a historical epigram. Gillies is the kind
of genius who leaves orders for “Gone, but not
forgotten,” to be inscribed upon his tombstone.
The Labour candidates at S. A. Legislative
Council April elections will be opposed by the
licensed victuallers. The latter point out that
although they supported the party for o.A.
Assembly last year, the Labour M’s.P. voted tor
Magarey, M.L.C.’s, drastic prohibition bill. Gut
of 17 Labour members in both Houses 12 are
teetotallers, and unless a greater proportion take
to drink the liquor interest knows them no more.
Dear Bulletin, —The purist who objects
to “I quite forgot all about it” is one of those
vexatious asses whose analytical faculty goes too
far, and yet not far enough. The best answer to
all such hypercritics is that the idiom of the
language requires and sanctions the use of a
host of expressions that will nob bear logical
analysis. Every time we say, “ How do you do f
we talk nonsense, but we talk English, for all
that!— Vkrbum.

A VAST CONTRACT.
UNCLE: “ Why did you come out to Australia?”
IT: “ Mother thought it would make a man of me.”
UNCLE : “Did she? Well, she’s given us a blessed tough job, anyhow.”
according to D.T. logic, evidently lies with the
party which got a minority of one. “ Practical
defeat ” is a good, elastic term, and simply means
that the Government had a close shave. In like
manner, if a bullet grazed the hat of Mr.
Brient it would be the practical death of the
D.T.’s editor.
Dundee legend with moral for Australia.
At Dundee, three years ago, two Labour candi-
dates were returned amid universal rejoicing.
One has since died, and the other was defeated,
the other day, with terrible slaughter by an old
opponent. Reporter interviews workman : “ Was
he not a satisfactory representative?” “ Oo —ah !
He was brawly —but, ye ken, he began to wear a
topcoat and a walkin’-stick and that sort o’ thing
wiuna do.” Verily, Keir Hardie is a wise man in
his generation.
Rev. Sparling, the retiring Primitive
Methodist president, is as inconsistent as the
ruck of Primitive Methodist presidents. In one
breath he says that “the legislative stream cannot
rise higher than its source, the ballot-box and
in the next he inveighs against the sale of drink
on Sunday and the Sunday evening admission-
charge to theatres, and urges that legislation
against these things should be enforced. How
can it be enforced when the majority of electors
do not believe in its enforcement ?
It is now seriously alleged that when the
gorgeous Cecil Rho'des cabled Ids proposition that
the Cape Colony should take up a few hundred
thousands of Australian debentures to help this
country through the panic, there was not the
spectre of a red cent in the Capetown treasury,
and if the offer had been accepted, Rhodes couldn’t
have found a coin. The swashbuckler was only
bluffing, in order to impress the Imperial autho-
rities with the greatness of his nigger-infested
province, and that bluff did a lot to help the
Matabele difficulty through.

When Sir Henry had concluded his denunciation of the

One of Victorian Minister Richardson’s
troubles in his recent tour was a deputation of
enraged agriculturists, who said they were paying
30s. a ton too much for the carriage of their wheat,
and that this result was mainly owing to the
nefarious system of Protection. Then Richardson
looked up some statistics, and pointed out that
they were only paying 18s. in all, and he blandly
asked how 18s. a ton could be 30s. a ton too much?
Whereupon the deputation’s jaw dropped heavily,
and, with a feeble remark that it had got its
information from a Freetrade politician who
lately visited the place, it rose and went out side-
ways.
B. R. Wise now spends most of his time
in explaining away that famous manifesto, where-
in the society of which he is president pledged
itself to a land-tax of 4d. in the £. B.R.W. now
asserts that what he wants and always wanted is
a land-tax of Id. in the £ only. The shortest
explanation is that 13. R. Wise lacks the courage
of his convictions. He has vague and un-
satisfied yearnings after a vigorous land-
tax policy, and, at the same time, he hasn’t
pluck enough to break away from the old
Freetrade revenue-tariff party, which is largely
made up of big landlords, who will go in
for a 500 per cent, protective tariff, or a general
repudiation of the national liabilities, or a spell of
anarchy, rather than pay 4d. in the £ on their
big estates. Therefore, Wise is now neither land-
taxer nor revenue-tariffist, but only a lawyer who
staggers feebly between two opinions and has no
visible faith in either.
Italy, judging by present signs, is going
to be a republic before long. The country is
bankrupt, rotten, and seething with discontent.
Its frantic efforts to be a Great Power have failed
dismally. Humbert has sent his little pit '>f
.£2,000,000 to London lest it should be seized
upon by his loyal subjects, and is seriously think-
ing of bolting after it himself while he and his
head are still connected. Queen Margherita spends
her days in “prayer and tears and abject terror.”
The Treasury is empty, the banks are broke, Sicily
isin chronic revolt, the bomb-thrower is abroad, the
army is believed to be discontented, and the Pope
cackles in the senile tones of old age at the
Vatican, and fancies that the temporal power of
the church may look up again before long, after
all. He is a sanguine kind of Pontiff; if he
understood the real nature of the situation he
would probably gather up his traps and bolt for
Peru instead of-waiting till the revolutionists call
for him with a notice of ejectment and fire the
Sacred College into the street.
Campbelltovm Herald’s advice to N.S.W,
electors; —
If professing Christians will vote for and support only
men who are known to have clean characters, they will be
doing a grand work. And they will be doing what they
have not hitherto done. It has been the other way about,
otherwise we should never have had in a Government two
of the lowest and most immoral men who stood in the
colony during the time they held office. One spent all
his spare time in a house of ill-fame, while the
other entertained a questionable character belonging to a
private bar of a Castlereagh-street hqtel. Yet time after
time we have seen the clergy, church-wardens, and Sun-
day-school teachers falling over each other in their desire
to shake the hand of the debauchee.
As every traveller knows, it is harder to
get a good cigar in the interior of Australia than
a glass of decent grog, and a glass of decent grog
is about as rare as teeth in a hen. Country readers
will therefore appreciate the innovation made by
a firm of well-known Sydney tobacconists, in
granting them facilities for-obtaining a box of
smokable cigars at city prices. An advt. in
another part of this issue, concerning the estab-
lishment of Messrs. M. and H. Woolf and Co.,
326 George-street, Sydney, will cast further light
upon the above statement.
M'Burney, Wallis & Co.’s Report.
As usual, we had a crowded attendance of buyers at
our wool sale to-day, and bidding up to a point was
very animated. The market may be quoted about equa'
to last week’s rates for all descriptions. Our catalogus
represented New England, Western, Southern, anc
Manaro wools. A few lots were passed in at prices
slightly under owner’s ideas of value, and private sales
will no doubt result. The folio ving brands are a few o)
those disposed of—WFM, from Tam worth ; A in diamond,
over Boree, from Uralla; JPG B, over Cargo, front
Orange; MMM, over Mudgee, from Mudgee; E, from
Tumut; JR, over R, from Rockley ;T. Burns, over Rock
ley, from Roskjey ; JD, over Z, over Walcha Road, from
Waloha; QM, over New England, from Uralla ; HFL, ovei
T, from Tumut; PR, scoured pieces, from Tumut; WS.
over R, washed, from Rockley.
We would advise owners to meet the market as soon as
possible, as many foreign buyers will shortly be leaving
our shoses, and, competition being lessened, must weaker
the market.
Our London agents have wired: “Sales opened with a
good attendance of buyers at prices equal to closing rate?
of last series.”
Sheepskins. -—The market is rather slack, especially foi
pelts, which sell somewhat lower. Best full-woolled, 4d
to 4|d ; medium, 3d to 3|d; pelts, Id to 2|d per lb.
Butchers’ greenskins: tops, 40d to 50d; seconds, 20d tc
30d ; pelts, Od to 18d each.
Hides were in brisk demand at our sale to-day ; best
heavies, 3d to 3Jd ; heavies, 2d to 2Jd: medium and light,
IJd to 2d ; dry, ltd to 2d per lb.
Tallow. —There was more disposition on the part ol
exporters to do business to-day, and an advance on all
shipping lines was noticeable. We quote nest mutton at
from £22 to £22 12s Od; best mixed, £2O 15s do £2l ;
trade parcels to £l9 10s per ton. r
Hair.— Horse, lOd to 13£d ; cow, 4jd to lOd ;: stump,
4id to 6Jd per lb.
Bones.— Shanks, £3 10s to £5 10s ; common, £1 10s to
£2 5s ; knuckles, £2 10s to £3 per ton.
Horns.— Large, £1 to £ll2s ; medium, 10s to £l; tips,
from 2s to 5s per 100.
Hoofs.—£2 to £3 per ton.

from £1 to £4 per ton for inferior to medium.
Sydney, 19th January, 1894.

MELBOURNE MATTERS.


Gold discoveries. Very rich fields have
been opened at Southern Cross, Coolgardie, Mur-
chison, &0., in West Australia, which will be one
of the greatest gold-producing countries of the
world. Land in Perth and Albany, the principal
cities, is ridiculously low in price, and must cer-
tainly increase very much in value in the next
few years. One of the best investments at present
time is to secure a few lots. The Intercolonial
Investment Co., Ltd., 309 Pitt-street, Sydney,
offers exceptionally well-positioned blocks from
.£lO each on very easy terms, or for cash,
guaranteed title. The company has been estab-
lished in Sydney and Perth for many years, and
is well known throughout the colonies for straight
dealing. The manager is Mr. W. J. Douglas,
vho will be pleased to forward plans to any
address, and, having a personal knowledge of the
properties, will select faithfully for intending
purchasers where required.

RESIST CONTEMPTIBLE IMITATIONS
Sociery
Writes a lady to The Bulletin
His wife is not always the cause of the Australian
citizen’s zeal for the “dear old mother country" and the
consequent title. The “ Lady ”of one brand-new “ Sir ”
has an exaggerated horror of anything like publicity,
and some years ago, when a friend hinted at the possi-
bility of her husband being placed in a prominent posi-
tion, she indignantly denied the insinuation, and ex-
pressed her wonder that any friend of hers should hint at
such a catastrophe.
Wanted—that lady’s name. . The Bulletin only
knows of one Australasian prominent politician’s
wife who would honestly work heart and soul to
save her husband from ennobblement—and, to do
her husband justice, he is so solid a Republican as
to be in no danger of taking a bribe. “ I would
almost as soon see him dead,” remarked that
model woman, “ for he might just as well be dead
as bo ticketed ‘Sold.’ ” Nor is tjie case one of
“ sour grapes,” for the Southern lawyer-politician
—not a South Australian or a Victorian —is a
most able and steadily-rising man whom the ’Ome
people would dearly love to ticket and then
laugh at.
• » •
The ensuing municipal elections at
Sydney’s pet marine suburb, Manly, turn mainly
on the question whether the proposed sewerage
scheme shall be carried out —at any rate, that is
the ostensible issue. Those who oppose a sewerage
system on account of its expense (though, as a
matter of fact, it would not be more costly than
the present filthy and otherwise offensive plan) are
simply in the position of people who are prepared
to di'iiy themselves and their fellow-citizens soap,
on the ground that it can’t be got for nothing.
The man who won’t wash is a savage ; the man
who won’t drain his town is a beast. The contest
at Manly is simply a fight between filth and
disinfectant. As usual, a large proportion of the
Town Pump interest is on the side of filth and
Thrift.
• • • • • • •
“Are your drains all right?” enquired the new
lodger of his landlady. “Oh, you needn’t be
afraid,” replied the old lady, “ we’ve not got no
drains here.” That probably occurred at Manly.
Diains or diphtheria ? That is the question.

A comparison of Sydney Daily Tele
graph’s article on the execution of Frances Knorr
and its articles goading on the brutal massacre of
the Mount Rennie _ boys illustrates how, in jour-
nalism as in politics, nimble minds may jump
from side to side of the widest ethioal chasms,
meeting the public after each jump with the faces
of men who act from deep and lasting convictions.

similar to the Star of India he provided, to he known as

Twinkle, twinkle, little star,
Dimming Southern Cross’s lustre,
Whilst across the seas afar
Minions of the Monarch muster.
Tinkle, joy-bells, gaily tinkle !
Beads of loyal sweat besprinkle
Beetled brows. O ! twinkle, twinkle,
Twinkle little star!
Twinkle, twinkle, gew-gaw small,
Dazzle eyes which booze is blearing;
Shine on legislators all
Who delight in “ bursts of cheering. '*
Tinkle, joy-bells ! Here’s a wrinkle
That we fondly hope and think’ll
Suit the Queen. O ! twinkle, twinkle,
Twinlde little star !
* • #
Coroner Ghoul’s violent verbal assault on
the witness Birtles, for telling unpleasant-sound-
ing truths upon oath concerning his relations with
the drowned girl Hannah M’Lennan, and thereby
blackening the character of the deceased, presents
yet another aspect. In her farewell letter—the
romantic type of suicide always leaves a farewell
letter—the girl (she was not an unsophisticated
girl, for she had been married before she
became acquainted with Birtles) wrote at
full length a damning indictment of Birtles for
having deceived her according to the usual fashion.
Everybody believes the pathetic letter of the
girl whose remains are lying at the Morgue
as a sad, though irrelevant evidence of her truth-
fulness, therefore if Birtles had denied the charge
brought against him he would have been jumped
on, not only by Coroner Ghoul, but by society at
large, as a man who was feebly trying to shield
his own repute by accusing a dead girl of gratuitous
falsehood. Also, he would possibly have been
run in for perjury, and in any case be would have
been contradicting a lady—which is bad manners,
to say the least of it. Even if lying is a virtue,
no man is expected to lie against a certainty, any
more than he is expected to bet on it.

“ A.8.C.” of the S.M. Herald, aggrieved
at the mental composure exhibited by the lately-
executed Mrs. Knorr, asserts that the assurances
of religion and the stimulations of brandy lead to
exactly the same mental condition. A philosophic,
but dangerous, line of speculation for a money-
making proprietary.

The most interesting Melb. divorce-case of
this season had its beginnings in a romantic
marriage of quite recent date. It is stated that
the girl eloped, in Sydney, letting herself down
from a third-storey window at night by a rope
of the bed-clothes.

It is creditable to Yictoiia that the “Alert
Wreck Relief Fund ” speedily, though unosten-
tatiously, caught up the Age “National” sub-
scription, which had a few days start. The news-
paper deserved to have a “ bad collection ” —to
use a religious term —because of its want of tact.
The Age should have arranged to send round a
magnanimous hat for Spciyht on the condition
that the Argus made a brotherly appeal for Mr.
D. Syme. Then the public might have been lured
into fueling truly sorry for both parties.
The fact that the now-hanged Mrs. Knorr
chose “ Gladys ” as a name for her baby will pro-
bably turn the tide again In favour of plain
“Susan” and “Jane” as girl nomenclature. At
present no mother who respects herself will ever
call her girl baby by any less romantic name than
“Beryl,” or “Phyllis,” or “Doris.” Conse-
quently, these names have drifted right down to
the gutter.*

Dear Bulletin, —lie the man with the
soundless footfall. An old V. D.L, ex-conviot
shoemaker, who had seen criminal life in all its
varieties, told me that when a thief, an assigned
servant, or a man who wanted to be “ unknown,”
gave him orders for shoes they were invariably of
the “ silent ” sort, made thus : the sole some inch
or so shorter than tiie upper leather, the latter,
with the toes of the foot well up, projecting over.
“No one,” he said, “could hear the approach of a
man wearing shoes thus made.”—Bush Lawyer.
“ Advertise, my
dear fellow; advertis-
ing is the only means
of success; modest
merit starves in a
garret; pushing self-
assertion builds itself
marble halls and fares
sumptuously every
day,” writes Mrs.
Alexander. She is
right. For example,
if you and your best
girl want a park-seat
which is partly occu-
pied by another
couple, don’t wait
about till they go
away. If you wait,
they will never go
away. Just march
right up and sit down
alongside them. They
will look very sour,
but they’ll soon
“clear.” Therein is
the whole philosophy
of getting on in the
world.

The Southern Cross (a Melb. religious
journal) has called upon Mr. Butler (of Sargood,
Butler and Nichol) to renounce the .£30,000
prize which he won in the Queensland Building
Society lottery. By accepting this huge windfall
in lieu of the rightful wages of sin—which is a
blank—the softgoods magnate is said to be luring
his own employes and young men in general to
destruction. Therefore, the Southern Cross
requests Mr. Butler to blot out his stroke of luck
and cast the .£30,000 prize ad rift upon an admiring
world, in which case the godly periodical will
“ recoup him the £lO he invested in this shame-
less speculation.” Butler, up to now, has remained
obdurate, but the spirited offer made by the
Southern Cross is bearing good fruit. A syndi-
cate of Adelaide commercials invested £2O in the
aforesaid lottery—result, one prize of £lO. On
the terms held out to Mr. Butler they are pre-
pared to repent at large and set a good example.
The syndicate despise their £lO prize now that
they have won it. With humble and contrite
hearts they ask to be recouped and cleansed of the
indiscretion.

Art in N.S.W. A pretty well-known
picture, by a well-known Sydney artist, was sold,
the other day in Sydney, for 4s. 6d. At the same
sale-rooms water-colours by fairly good painters
were disposed of at from Is. 3d. to 2s. 6d. each.
• * »
The Military Tournament, which was held
for alleged charitable purposes at Moore Park,
Sydney, three months ago, winds up, accord-
ing to a published statement, in abject
fashion. The takings were £1367 odd, and the
balance left for charities is something like £l2.
Among other riotous items of expense wore £476
for prizes; rent, £204; preparing ground, £125;
printing and stationery, £271; catering, &c,,
£275. In any decently-managed show the right
of catering is sold for a considerable sum, but the
Tournament committee, instead of selling it, seem
to have paid someone to take the thing away.
Also it is solemnly alleged that two firms offered
to supply printing and stationery free, condition-
ally on being allowed the sole right to sell pro-
grammes, and the offer was loftily declined as
beneath the dignity of the full-blown officer.
However that may be, the show that professes to
be for charitable purposes, and which pans out for
£l2 of charity and £1355 worth of military pomp
and gorgeousness, is a show that travels upon
false pretences. Next time the gorgeous officers
had better engage Goodman or Rickards to run
their show on business principles, or else sell the
whole tournament for a lump sum to the Coogee
Aquarium man.

Up to last Saturday the Speight testi-
monial was still keeping well ahead of the Syme
testimonial, the figures being : Speight, £749 195.;
Syme, £671 13s. But the Age still points out
that its testimonial proves conclusively that the
great soul of the country is with it, and whoops
up the Subscriptions with the energy of a man
urging a lame bullock uphill in a dry country.

MISTAKEN,

self, as shouldn’t, there ain’t a

“Lady Monro” was doing Hunter-street,
Newcastle, recently, where site met a mild-looking
youth balancing liimself on one leg at a boot-
black’s stand. He was a good specimen of the
summer dude —soft felt hat parted in the middle,
with a pudding-cloth wound round it; a bit of
glass in his eye; vest missing; p'enty of white
shirt showing ; and a red silk belly-band, in lieu
of braces, folded, stage-pirate-like, round the top
of white duck*pants— those stiff canvas things that
small boys “ quack ” at. His get-up appeared to
annoy her ladyship, who lowered herhead, making
a “ keb ” at him, goat-fashion, landed her flat-
tened, greasy, black bonnet somewhere about his
rits, and knocked him off his perch. Then she
planted her own foot thereon, and ordered a shine
for the big toe peeping out of her boot. When
Johnnie had gathered himself together and fixed
the window in his eye, he feebly remonstrated
with her in his own drawling fashion. My lady
was astonished; she “didn’t know it was alive,”
she said. Then, reaching out, she took from his
unresisting hand a bag of cherries, and zigzagged
down the street to give another entertainment.
The fun was that, being a new-ehum, the gentle-
man was unaware that he had been battered about
by a dilapidated scion of true nobility from dear
old England, and cited the incident at length, as
an awful example of beastly colonial manners.
• • #
Quong Tart’s fellow-countrymen indig-
nantly condemn his conduct in abetting the anti-
opium crusade in Sydney. They regard the
crusade as springing from racial antipathy more
than from an intelligent knowledge of the uses of
the drug, and the sight of Tart in the vanguard
makes them bite their pig tails.
* * ♦
Dear Bulletin, —Dr, Ferriss’ statements
before the Indian Opium Commission will sur-
prise nobody who has dwelt where opium-smoking
is endemic. There is more opium per head used
in Singapore than in any other place out of China;
yet, during a three years’ residence, I never saw
anything approaching the “opium-wreck” we
hear so much about. The drug may possibly
damage the white man more than the yellow, but,
moderately used, it does the Chow no injury that
I could discover. The “immoderate-use” argu-
ment applies no more to opium than to tobacco—
or, for that matter, to tea —the excessive use of
which is simply murderous.—Equatorial.

ANOTHER VIEW OF IT.
(FOR THE BULLETIN.)
In a cannibal isle a cannibal king
With his cannibal suite were banqueting
On babies pickled and babies fried,
And child’s head stewed with the brains inside,
And babies roasted ; the choicest course
Was babies served up with oyster sauce.
And as he toyed with a “ bit of wing,”
The chief remarked to the gobbling king :
“They’ve hanged, I’ve heard, down in Melbourne
town
A woman of somewhat wide renown.
Her game, it seems, till by hanging marred,
Was burying kids in her own backyard.”
The king remarked : “ Serve her right, I say,
For squandering food in that sinful way.”

upon a girl of 13, the prisoner, a Salvation Harmy

Hobart Exhibition is to be withheld until
the end of the year, so as to give George and May
a chance to “be present at the inaugural cere-
monies.” The blatant stupidity of Premier Pat-
terson has infected the whole hemisphere. Every
do°- and poultry show and bush race-meeting
will be getting itself indefinitely postponed against
the joyful visit of a royal pair who don’t dream,
and never dreamt, and never will dream of
coming. If they do so dream it is a horrible night-
mare.
# « *
A Chinaman, being consigned to Albury
gaol for trying to evade the poll-tax, soon sickened
and died of typhoid fever, a disease almost un-
known to Chows in their naturally filthy state.
Pigs, they say, develope small-pox if frequently
washed.
* » *
Cheerful item from Daylesford Advocate
(Vic.):-
Soarcity of money has forced many country people to
the old-fashioned system of barter. Daylesford butchers
have been exchanging carcases of pigs and sheep for
chaff; drapers have been giving blankets and suits for
firewood, and clergymen have even performed the cere-
mony of marriage in exchange for a stated quantity of
gravel and garden-manure.
* # ft
Plucky young Crawcour, of Williamstown,
died bravely fighting for the pawnbrokery, but
one would much rather have seen him spared,
while the loot went. The funeral was tremendous,
and was the only case in which a religious service
at the synagogue has thrilled Melbourne.
# # •
In Parkes (N.S.W.) recently a married
couple grew simultaneously tired of one another.
An obliging friend took the wife home and gave a
horse, cart and harness to boot. The husband
harnessed the horse to the cart, and is travelling
in search of another partner. Note the name of
the place where these things happen—Parkes !
You never heard of anything like that at Glad-
stone.

Dear Bulletin, —Since Government will
keep on flogging, why not, with a view to revenue,
tike the floggees’ yells down in the phonograph,
and then place the instrument in convenient
spots, with a notice affixed something like this : —•
“Put a penny in the slot and hear Maguire at the
triangles. Three yells and a groan for one penny.
Splendid assortment of curses, groans, screams,
howls and prayers, etc. Very shrill. Purveyors
to His Excellency Sir R. Duff,” etc., etc. —
Boiling Billy.
# * *
In a little Monaro township, Sunday ser-
vice is conducted by a local land-agent, who acts
as lay-reader. One member of the church, with
a weakness for old clothes and plenty of tobacco
on “the Lord’s Day,” excuses himself from attend-
ing service upon the ground that a civil servant is
not a fit person to conduct it, as “ A man cannot
serve God and the Government too.”
* « *
A Sydney lady is in need of a little
assistance. She wants to write a novel, and is
“quite sure that if someone would just arrange
the plot, write up the scenery and arrange the
characters, she could manage the conversations
quite easily.” A most womanly woman.
* # *
Says N.S.W. grog-secretary Bevill: “Pros-
perity is drink’s twin brother.” Yes, sir ; and so
is adversity. We drink because it is a hot day.
We drink because it is a cold day. We drink
when we feel particularly well, and also when we
feel particularly ill. -

In Auckland (M.L.), a young man is taking
all the legal proceedings he can get hold of, his
grievance being that his wife, aged 21, has eloped
with his father, aged 68. It was probably the
beautiful shine of the old man’s bald head which
led her astray.

journal the other day there were 180 applications !
Few Brisbane reporters, by the way, now get over
£2 a week.

lias been photographed with the members of Gov.

# # *
A free-and-easy damsel, writing in Pall
Mall Gazette on Chinese dressmakers in Japan,
artlessly remarks :
He (the Chow person), when I was drinking my cup of
coffee in bed, appeared in answer to my summons. It
was far from a pleasant feeling, and distinctly creepy,
having his long, highly-polished, aristocratic nails wan-
dering over my neck and arms, with bird-like talons at
least an inch long.
Woman in want of a dress will do, or endure, any-
thing.
ft * •
A Sydney lady has invented a new variety
of the “late-lamented” business. Her second
husband being absent when the photographer
came to “take” their residence, she hung up
conspicuously on the front verandah-post an
enlarged, framed portrait of her first husband,
posed herself alongside, and had the photo, taken!
saw the result must have been akin to those
experienced by the first husband when, from the
shades of the Unknown, lie saw his vacant place
filled a few mbffths after his arrival there.
How is it that no man ever hangs up in
his second, wife’s house the portrait of his first
wife ; whereas it is quite an exceptional thing not
to find the portrait of a woman’s first husband
hung conspicuously in the house of her second
husband ? Is it that women are so much more
tasteful than men ? Or so much more enduringly
affectionate?

A Beech worth (Vic.) man was reported
missing, and a long search for him was made,
darns dragged, and holes explored, ere he was
found. To the police belongs the honour of his
discovery—he was serving a sentence in the local
gaol.
# # #
Dear Bulletin,— A man—the happy
daddy of a large family—living within sight of
my office window, always keeps a pet monkey.
One died recently, but he purchased another
almost immediately. I had some idle curiosity as
to the utility of the animal, till I saw it operating
in a business-like and very natural manner on the
heads of his urchins. This may contain a valuable
tip to the heads of families, and the hairy man of
Samoa. —Yours, Snell. P.S. — A school-master
tells me no family should be without one. —S.
* » *
Sydney Municipal Council declined the
proffered portrait of a deceased alderman, fearing
lest a precedent being thus established the Town
Hall would be filled with the pictures of dead
aldermen, and be thus converted into a chamber
of horrors. Sufficient unto the chamber are the
live horrors thereof.

At Brunswick (Melb.) the other day a lady
sued another lady for 30s. money owing for
“washing and laying out” the corpse of the
latter’s husband. The gruesome case was dis-
missed.

* * *
It got abroad lately that an important
Gippsland railway-station would be placed in
charge of a lady. The people were all up in arms
and a powerful deputation waited on the Minister,
who told them not to yell before they wore hurt.
However, she took charge and has proved a
brilliant success. She could not have become so
successful without the enthusiastic help of her
opponents.
* « #
At the beginning of the recent Xmas
vacation, 13 male and female teachers arrived in
Sydney, bag and baggage, from Broken Hill, with
the avowed intention never to return to that
thirsty district. They spent most of their month’s
holiday at the Department of Public Instruction,
beseeching for removal to some other country
town. There is always a mild rebellion among
country teachers, after vacations, against returning
to the out-back schools —but Broken Hill is their
particular horror. Teachers—unmarried female
ones especially —regard an appointment up that
way as a thing to be abjured by all possible
means; and those already there move heaven and
earth to get away. The particular consignment
aforesaid clubbed together and hired railway
carriages from Broken Hill to Sydney, thereby
saving about .£3 each. It costs the greater part
of a month’s cheque to visit Sydney from Broken
Hill, beside the loss of a week on the return trip.
Appointments Broken Hill way carry a slight
increase of salary, but the extra local cost of living
there more than balances the bonus. To all these
grievances lady teachers add an extra one. The
fine red dust—the principal component of the
atmosphere at Broken Hill—spoils linen, and they
have to wear turkey-red underclothing. This
objection is inexplicable, for turkey twill is at
least as comfortable as linen, and looks don’t
count, for nobody sees. Broken Hill is the N.S.W.
Dept, of Public Instructions, bogey-man. Many
a giddy teacher has been frightened into sobriety
by the mere threat of an appointment there.

In 1892, Victoria made 1,554,130 gallons
wine, and shipped 222,418 gallons ; S.A. produced
913,107 gallons, and exported 325,035 gallons;
whilst N.S.W. made 801,835 gallons, and exported
27,856 gallons.
Casey’s Hotel, corner George and Liver-
pool streets, Sydney, was pulled down the other
day, and the material sold. A man bought a
single girder for 6s. Last week, when he under-
took to reduce it, he noticed an old tin-plate tacked
on one face, and on removing it withdrew from this
“safe deposit” a bundle of old B.N.S.W, and
Commercial Bank notes amounting to .£IOOO.
Prof. Lornbroso, the Italian specialist on
crime, has just completed a work on “Criminal
Woman,” a companion to his epoch-making book
on “Criminal Man,” He says that immoral or
criminal women are shorter in the arms, longer in
the hands and feet, heavier in the body, smaller in
the head, larger in the face, and darker in the
hair and eyes than normal women. Prostitution
he regards as identical with criminality. Tattoo-
ing -is an infallible indication of criminal ten-
dencies.
The girls’ new craze, this turning of rings
on the fingers of young men, is thus explained :
If a young lady meets a young man with a ring
on his finger, she is to turn the ring two or three
times. Then with another man the same thing,
and so on until she has turned rings to the extent
of about twenty-four times. Then the next thing
to do is to look for a married person, male or female,
wearing a marriage-ring. This ring she is to turn
twice, and the next man she shakes hands with
will be her husband.
Every Victorian hangman has been
blighted by his billet. The first, old “Jack
Harris,” was threatened with death for hanging
the murderers of John Price, and cleared out, too
frightened to stay and hang anyone. His
successor, Bamford, had his eye knocked out in a
row in which he was attacked because he was
what he was. Gately, who followed, led a dog’s
life, miserable in gaol and the victim of the
fiendish torments of larrikins when out. He
“cleared” rather than hang Ned Kelly, whose
friends threatened the executioner with dire
vengeance. Upjohn, however, performed the job,
and got knocked down immediately after. How
it happened was in this wise. Some prisoner-
wardsmen were brought out of their cells just after
the execution to “clean up.” One of these walked
straight up to Upjohn, and planted a straight-
frorn-the-shoulder staggerer in the hangman’s face,
with the remark : “Take that, you ——, for hang-
ing Ned Kelly !” Last on the list is Jones.

Jane Calap.
The public may remember the account of Mrs
Mills’ illness and recovery, published some time
ago. We are glad that Mrs. Calap heard of it and
went straight to that lady herself for the infor-
mation she wanted. The visit resulted just as
might have been expected. Both our good friends
had suffered from the same disease, indigestion
and dyspepsia, and the remedy which cured in the
first case was equally successful in that of her
neighbour. No wonder Mrs. Calap had lost
all faith in physic, and if Mother Seigel’s Syrup
were “ physic,” we should not look for people to
have faith in it either. But it is a remedy, not
“ physic.” Itdoesn’t upset and disgust, it soothes
and heals. Men fall ill, to be sure, but women bear
most of the pain in this sad world, and when once
acquainted they and “ Mother Seigel” are “ever
the best of friends,” like Joe and Pip in Dickens’
story.

round the joints AA, round the instep 88, round heel CO,
round the ankle DD ; next ascertain the length and width
of tread by placing the foot on a sheet of paper, and
running a lead pencil round the heel and toes. Cut the
outline thus made with a scissors, and enclose with the
other measurements, stating what size you generally take,
what are your peculiarities—a weak ankle, perhaps a pet

Parson Campbell, who wrote to the papers
explaining that Mrs. Knorr ought to be hanged
because “ she was now resigned,” recalls an old
story of little Father Lordon, R.O. Chaplain to
Melb. Gaol many years ago. A condemned
criminal whom his reverence had made “perfectly
resigned,” was reprieved at the last moment.
“Ah,” sighed Father Lordon, “I am not so
very pleased, after all—he will never be so well
prepared again.” It was the same priest who, to a
sturdy Irishman who came to give him notice of
marriage, exclaimed: “Why, Pat, your wife’s
only dead two months.” “And isn’t she as dead
»s iver she’ll be ? ” retorted Patrick.
A curious financial item comes from Vic-
toria. According to the newspaper report, a man
presented a cheque, purporting to be signed by a
local firm, at the bank, and duly received the
amount. Then the firm was much astonished to
learn that its account was overdrawn, and de-
nounced the cheque as a forgery, and reported the
matter to the police. The police thereupon
started after the forger, but failed to find him,
and a day or two later the firm came
along, and acknowledged in the handsomest
manner that the charge was a mistake and
that the cheque was no forgery, • after all.
Apparently the matter dropped there. Mean-
while, if the police had found the man in question,
and the firm hadn’t recovered its lost memory, the
sentence would probably have been three years in
gaol at the lowest estimate. There is no evidence
of any malicious intent in the matter, but if there
had been any malicious intent —well, about the
easiest way to get rid of an enemy is to give him
a cheque in a charitable spirit, and then have him
run in for forging the document. When one man
in the dock swears that a signature is genuine,
and one man and an expert in the witness-box
swear that it is not, there is never any doubt about
the result. The necessary expert can always be
obtained, and if the signature is so very like the
real thing that nobody but the expert can tell the
difference, that merely proves that the prisoner is
an exceedingly dangerous forger, and if he hasn’t
been previously convicted he ought to have been,
and as a consequence he gets two years extra.
Australasian Medical Gazette congratu-
lates Dr. Grace Robinson upon her appointment
as Resident Medical Officer to the newly-estab-
lished Maternity Branch of Sydney Benevolent
Asylum, “the more so as she has had to contend
against no less an important factor—or factors,
rather—than the Dean of the Faculty of Medicine
of the Sydney University, the Professor of Physi-
°logy, the President of the Board of Health, and
the Medical Adviser to the Government, who,
individually and collectively, opposed this lady
physician’s appointment to a small salaried posi-
tion. For the information of such readers as may
not happen to be initiated, we must explain that
the above appointments referred to are all held by
one man Professor Anderson Stuart.” The
Gazette, after hinting that Professor Stuart could
do worse than stick to his University duties and
pay, instead of reaching out all round for more,
remarks that, “If distinctions are to be drawn
between the sexes when it comes to a division of
spoils, especially in favour of the men, then the
lady medical students ought to be charged smaller
fees, and should get modified diplomas only,”
There is much hard explanation going on
just now in connection with the “Free Metho-
dist” Church at Ballarat (Vic.) Pastor John-
son left this church some time ago, and the Con-
ference, instead of the customary certificate, gave
him a brief document setting forth that it couldn’t
recommend him to any other congregation.
Nevertheless, the fallen preacher turned up at
Auckland with a considerably different kind of
document, and got on well until the discrepancy
between the two papers attracted attention.
Then he returned to Ballarat and has been
explaining ever since. The average parson is
probably the worst explainer on the face of
the earth, and Mr. Johnson seems to be a trifle
worse than the average. His explanation wanders
over the facts that he was converted at the age of
15 and had “won hundreds of souls;” that his
wife believed in faith-healing and died of internal
cancer; that he left Ballarat by the 6.25 a.m.
train “yet even at that early hour a large con-
course of people assembled on the platform in
tears,” that he was very ill on the voyage through
mental strain and sea-sickness ; and that he had
intended the signature on the alleged bogus docu-
ment to be only type-written, only the operator
jerked the paper from the machine before he got to
the signature so that it had to be done in ink.
Also Pastor Johnson acknowledged that he had
made a false step, but had since confessed it
“with tears.” “Had not,” he asked, “the cele-
brities of Scripture sinned and obtained forgive-
ness? Noah had been drunk. (Laughter.) Abra-
ham had deceived. (Laughter.) Jacob had
robbed his brother. (Laughter.) David com-
mitted adultery. (Uproar.) Peter had sworn
and lied.” Then the meeting, after showing
symptoms of a free fight, closed with the doxo-
logy.

F. E. Allan, the great bowler of years ago, has
taken to bowling—which is quile another matter—in his
middle age. Last week he was beaten in the final heat
for the “championship of Warniambool green.” Think
of it! Once Frank speared lions. Now he catches flies.
We shall all catch flies one day.

SPORTING NOTIONS-
Mark how the V.R.O. closes up its ranks, to
charge to the Flat.
# • •
The Y.R.O. guarantees .£11,210 in stakes for
the four days' autumn meeting at Flemington.
• * *
Westralia has its Carbine, and the owner claims
£lOOO damages from a local newspaper for an alleged
libellous suggestion that the horse ran “stiff” recently.
Nobody could make remarks of that sort about the real,
original Carbine.
# * •
Perhaps the strangest and most meritorious
point of Peter Jackson’s record is the fact that after
Keeping a hotel he won first-class fights. As a rule, the
pub. is the grave of the champion’s reputation.
# * *
The powerful influence of the Sydney book-
makers is clearly responsible for the banishment from
the province of the monster consultation and the squelch-
ing of the small totes. The latter, at any rate, deserve
extinction, yet it is doubtful whether they are any worse
than most of the Tommies who would crucify them. But
let the bookmakers beware lest in striving for a monopoly
of “the mugs’ ” money they introduce the regular totalis-
ator as they did in other provinces.
# • *
No wonder sculling and boxing have gone down
in Sydney. By taking a penny trip to Balmain, one can
now witness an exciting contest between the rival ferry
services, with the chance at the finish of a stand-up fight
between the captains. Then companies, unlike athletes,
instead of compromising, always “ make it willing.”
# # *
By winning the Nursery Handicap at Rosehill
with the Nordenfeldt two-year-old, First Shot, Ike Earn-
shaw began his winning account for Mr. Sam Hordern.
# * #
The bookmakers at Sydney Tatt’s. have resented
the innovation of “reading the card.” Bookmakers are
very conservative ; so, for that matter, are all people who
make money by methods more easy than nice.
* * »
Sullivan is going to end like all scullers—Bill
Beach excepted. He is now host of the Rutland Hotel,
Hammersmith (Eng.).
# # •
After his recent brilliant successes in Maoriland
young Gormley (a son of the senior M.L.A. for the Mur-
rumbidgee) should try his luck against the English and
American aquatic amateurs.
« • •
Titan, a horse of many vicissitudes, may fulfil
early promise, and atone for subsequent disappointments,
by winning the Newmarket Handicap with the crushing
weight of lost. lib.
• * «
Two of the most prominent members of Tatter-
sail’s, Brisbane, who have hitherto conducted five-shilling
consultalions locally, have amalgamated this portion of
their businesses, and propose extending their operations
outside Bananaland. “ Billy” Mooney and “ Jem” Nes-
bit announce in another column their opening programme.
As all their past sweeps have been drawn openly, in
the now acknowledged centre of consultation businesses,
the public can rely upon a square deal.
» * «
“Foxall” Keene was the most successful
American horse-owner of last season. His 1893 winnings
were nearly ±60,000.
* * a
At all events they are candid in N.Q. The
N.Q. Racing Association, moving to suppress consulta-
tions, expressly declares that it does not take action on
grounds of morality. It believes sweeps injure racing.
They certainly injure bookmakers.
* * *
Fitzsimmons has less money than he had, and is
anxious enough for another job. Larry Foley is about
the only pug. of the lot who has been able to| stand his
oats.
* * *
Taking the line through Laing, Slavin, at his
best, should have made mince-meat of Goddard. But at
boxing, as in other things, “ three cornered form ” is
deceptive.
• * #
Billy McCarthy has retired from the ring.
Like Dempsey, he was a good man in his class who had
the misfortune to run against Fitzsimmons, a big man
whom nature had furnished with the thews of a giant but
who, having little flesh, could scale as a middle-weight.
# # •
At N. Fitzroy, Melb., a girl with £16,000 has
just married a jockey boy, who has not got higher than
“ riding work.” Kinsman made them one.
• • *
Melbourne University beat Sydney at cricket
by an innings and 39 runs. Melbourne made 411 in first
innings, and the result was never in doubt.
» • •
The N.S.W. Minister for Justice has frightened
even the big Sydney totes. The biggest of them declined
to do business last Saturday.
* # •
Laing, Goddard’s victor, is now under Larry
Foley’s tuition, which makes Joseph’s chance of reversing
the last decision much smaller.
# • «
Mr. Macpherson, the crack Australian amateur
runner, has gone to America for a twelve months’ trip.
It is to be hoped “ Billy ” will give the Yanks a taste of
his running quality whilst in the States.
* * »
Griffo, unable to get on a fight, is now reported
to be workiiigforalivingin America Perish the thought!
* « •
A fashionable Sydney jockey now hangs his
hands like a kangaroo’s fore-paws whilst doing his pre-
liminaries, in order to show his diamonds.
# * #
They all drop into the same haven. Mick Rush
was a long time over it, but be has got there at last. He
now runs Milthorpe’s old hotel in Sydney.
• * •
The Royal Agricultural Society of N.S.W.
holds its metropolitan show from 21st to 27th March.
Entries close Saturday, 24th February, and schedules
with full particulars may be had from secretary Webster,
Tatiersall’s Chambers, Hunter-street, Sydney. Gov. Duff
will open the show at noon on 22nd March. £l5OO is
given in prizes.

“THE LAST OF SIX.
German Joe.
(FOR THE BULLETIN.)
Skirting the swamp and the tangled scrub,
Trumping and turning amidst the trees,
Carrying nothing but blankets and grub,
Hiking no heed of his health or ease,
Hither and thither with never a goal,
Heavy, and solemn, and stiff, and slow,
Seeking a track and a long-lost line,
“ Blazed avay to dob lead of mine ”
Restless and ricketty German Joe.
Down in the gully and up the range,
Stung by the gale and the hate-hot sun.
Never a greeting to give in change,
Never a tip from the nearest run,
Seeking a guide to a golden hole.
Lost in the lone land long ago,
Left in the keep of the hills and trees—
Jealous to have and to hold are these,
Hope you may get it, though, German Joe,
‘Likely old yarn for a darned marine !
Struck it, you say, at the river head—
Back where the bellowing bunyip’s seen,
Out beyond everywhere—rich and red;
Left it for tucker, and lost the track,
Blazed till your arm couldn’t strike a blow.
Gravel that gleams with the golden stuff,
Nuggets ‘shust like as der plums in duff.’
What are you giving us, German Joe?”
“bHze? Yes ; you strike for the Granite Stair’
Make to the left when you cross the creek,
South till you meet with a monkey bear,
1 Tramp in his tracks for about a week;
Then you can travel the sky-line back.
So long, old chap, if you’re bound to go.
Don’t you forget when you’re rich and great
Who laid you on to the lost lead, mate—
Mad as a hatter is German Joe.”
Laugh as they may, they will stand his friends,
Right as rain when the old man takes
Down to his bunk in the hut, and spends
Seven weeks fighting the fever and shakes,
Muttering still of his lucky lead :
“ Vhisper—l leds you all in der know,
Den you pe richer nor as der pank.”
Boys, he’s a man if he is a crank—
Whisky and physic for German Joe.
Now he’s abroad in a wild dream-land,
Baring his breast to the river breeze—
Out where the rock-ribbed ranges stand,
Whispering his tale to the secret trees
Hither and fro with a phantom’s speed,
Over the plains where the mad winds blow.
Cover his face now, and carve a stone,
Henceforth his spirit must seek alone—
Dead as a door-nail is German Joe.
Bushmen have yarned of a ghost that went
Blazing a track from the Granite Stair
Down to a shaft and a tattered tent,
Many days’ journey from anywhere.
Others have said that the bushmen lied.
Liars or not, it is true, we know,
Men have discovered a golden mine
Out in the track of an old blazed line,
Led by the spirit of German Joe.
Melb.
EDWARD DYS[?]N.
Why does a man whisper in another’s ear
when no one is within earshot?
Humboldt mentions a South American
parrot which spoke in an extinct Indian language.
The Geelong bar has been swept aivay.
Fifty years ago this would have made Geelong
Melbourne.
Nelly Wick, aged eight, barber’s daughter,
has been displaying to delighted audiences at the
London Aquarium her accomplishment of shaving
13 men in 10 minutes.
Every intelligent man considers, with
more or less reason, that every other man is a
fool in most things, but very few people are quick
to perceive things in which the other man is wiser
than themselves.
Dear Bulletin, —Mountebank Stead’s
drink-cure—like every other nostrum of the sort—
must be a fraud, simply because it begins at the
wrong end of the stick. There may be boozers-
for-boozing’s-sake who can be chemicalled into
temporary abstinence, but where it is the brain—
not thestomach—thatcraves the alcoholic fillip, the
only way to fortify the man against his foe is to
re-build his nervous system—a work to which no
drug in the pharmacopoeia can be more than a
doubtful adjunct.— Been There.
Dear Bulletin, —Well-fed clergymen are
continually preaching about the everlasting hell
in store for us. Put one of the Hell-preachers
into a 6 x 8 tent, on the bank of a river, where
mosquitoes, bull-ants, spiders, centipedes, and
perhaps a stray scorpion or two keep him up,
horror-stricken, all night striking matches and
looking at himself to see where he was bitten last,
and then next morning let him find his meat fly-
blown ; his sugar a living mass of ants; his flour
black with miscellaneous abominations, and both
his fishing-lines in the river snagged ! And then
see whether he’d preach any more about a hell
hereafter / 6xB.
The ex-King of Naples, who still lives, is
said to have been the last Italian monarch to
claim the virtues of a patent medicine. His royal
touch was commonly supposed to cure all com-
plaints in his faithful subjects—consumption,
pains in the back, measles, loss of memory, small-
pox, weak eyes, or any disease that they could
think of. His Majesty was careful not to do
much laying on of hands, all the same. He
picked his patients with wise discretion. If a
bouncing damsel stood around the palace gates,
suffering from an indefinable ailment, then the
King, perhaps, would touch her under the chin and
effect a marvellous cure.

new and successful “Cannalin” treatment, 257
George-st., close to Bridge-st., Sydney.*
Dear Bulletin, —At Kew Gardens, the
other day, wandering through the splendid con-
servatories, I was surprised to see the familiar
lantana —in Queensland a pest which divisional
boards and private persons pay hundreds of
pounds to extirpate—carefully grown in a pot
with the other hothouse plants. “ What is that ? ”
I asked one of the gardeners. “That comes from
Africa, sir—lantana is the name, sir.” “Is it
valuable?” “ Well, I dare say those plants are
worth 7s. fid. each, sir.” “Those plants ” were
scrubby, little specimens about six inches high.
Can nobody open up an Australian export trade
in lantana?—Yours, Australian.

The writing of the Caligraph can always be recognised
by its peculiar evenness and regularity of impression ;
this is due to its being the only typewriter which writes
on a Hat surface.
The Electric Telegraph Department of N.S. Wales, after
testing every machine in the market, has just given the
Agei t for the Caligraph the largest order ever given in
Australia in one line for Typewriters.
There are now 120 Caligraphs in the Government
Departments of N.S. Wales. There are more Caligraphs
in the public offices of Australia than machines of all
other makes combined.
The Caligraph will manifold 20 copies at one operation
by interleaving carbon, and when used in conjunction
with Edison’s Mimeograph will produce 1500 copies from
one stencil, each equal to the best original work. Send
for Mimeograph circular.
The New Special No. 3 Caligraph has two interchange-
able platens, a lateral ribbon movement, and six addi-
tional keys which may be fitted with fractions or other
characters to order.
