JTJHIS Company is being formed to take over, as a
i going concern, the business, now, and for the past
fourteen years, carried on by Messrs. BEALE & CO.
in Sydney, and throughout New South Wales, as Manu-
facturers, Importers, and Distributors of Sewing Machines
and Musical Instruments ; also the Stock-in-trade, Bal-
ances, and all debts due to the firm ; the Machinery,
Plant, and all materials.
The concern has branches and travellers all over the
territory. Its success is so well-known that comment
is needless ; suffice it to say that the business will be run
on the same lines in the future that have ensured con-
tinued success in the past, and the extended confidence
between the present firm and its customers will be
studiously maintained.
The volume of business has steadily grown, and during
five years the trade has totalled the enormous amount of
1,178, while the profits have been large and steadily
maintained. By the system of liens on goods sold until
fully paid for, the losses by bad debts have been reduced
to a comparatively insignificant amount.
With the business there will be handed over the
following properties; the title and interest in the present
firm of the freehold land and buildings situated at 482
and 484 George Street, Sydney, immediately opposite the
splendid edifice now being erected for the Central City
Markets. These buildings fotm a striking feature in the
centre of the busiest block in the city, and for solidity
and quality of finish are unsurpassed. The portions not
required by the firm are let to substantial tenants.
Also the Factory and Stores at Nelson Street, Annan-
dale, which occupy freehold land 30 feet by 200 feet, and
leasehold 60 feet by 400 feet. The principal Factory,
with engine and machinery, stands upon the freehold. All
the machinery is specially applicable to the work ; there
are ample supplies of seasoned timber for future require-
ments. Finally the freehold brick store and stables Myrtle
Street, city. All these properties are taken over at cost.
Stocks and Plant at valuation ; and customer's balances
with allowance for contingencies.
Messrs. Beale & Co. take the whole of their interest in
shares; no good-will is demanded; no preference or
promoters shares. Mr. Octavius C. Beale will act as
Managing Director for five years at least.
All the firm’s Trade Marks, will become the property
of the Company ; and the system of protecting goods by
liens until fully paid for, which has stood the test of years
will be strictly adhered to.
The results contemplated in converting the busines
into a Company are—(l) Expansion by introduction of
additional capital ; (2) Co-operation of interest amongst
a wide-spread body of shareholders ; (3) lire seeming of
perpetuity of the business.
A sound progressive concern is offered, the results of
which in pa-t years justify the expectation of 10 to 12 j
per cent, dividends, besides providing Reserves.
Full prospectus and information may be obtained from
the following agents, who are authorized to receive de-
posits on shares.

“ But who is to judge as to when one has
reached the stage where it is unsafe for him to
drink more? This is left to the man who is

Wyndham, W.A., holds the prison-break-
ing championship. In the gaol (calculated to hold
25) there were GO aboriginal prisoners. One of
them, anxious to spend Xmas in his native humpy,
stole the keys, and unlocked his own and 32 other
aborigines’ neck and leg irons. Then they shifted
two sheets of galvanised iron and walked out.
Three days afterwards 25 more thus departed,
leaving on the hands of the gaoler but two dod-
dering old men, too blind to get away.
Editor Bulletin, — lie mirage controversy.
Let me draw attention to an extraordinary mystery
not four miles from here, which is commonly
known as “ O’Brian’s Fire.” In the centre of a
thick clump of pine timber, on the open plain,
there is plainly visible any dark night a column
of glaring red flames. No matter from whab
point of the compass you approach, when you get
within a radius of forty yards the flames suddenly
vanish. The closest examination fails to reveal
anything unusual in the surroundings. Scores of
people have been puzzled, and many of your
readers may remember visiting the place.—Yours,
“A.” Narandera, N.S.W.
The Moravian missionaries at Batavia
River (N.Q.) are simple, untutored children of
light. The Torres Straits paper lately hinted
that the police were prevented from making
reprisals for attacks on whites by the Batavia
River blacks, lest mission interests should suffer.
The Moravian missionary in charge, finding he had
no ground for a libel-action, the thing being true
enough, wrote to the editor thus :
I thought first to bring an action for defamation against
you. But you, as an editor, are protected. I would have
to commence proceed! rigs atTownsville. land my brother
missionary, Mr. Ward, cannot afford time and money for
that. But I tell you what I will do. I will call you a
liar, as you are, and then you can summon me here for
defamation. Neither I nor Mr. Ward wish to avoid meet-
ing your charges. We give you the lie direct, and
challenge you to prove what you have said ■, gainst us in
open court. lam not an Englishman born, but I like
English fair play, and I invite you to prove that in this
matter you are not a liar. I say you are.—N. Hey,
Moravian Missionary at Mapoon, Batavia River.

DOMESTIC ECONOMY!
WHAT IS IT ?
DOMESTIC ECONOMY is primarily the art) of feeding and clothing the family at as low a rate as possible,
without impairing the family digestion, or taking away the character of its members for being well and com-
fortably dressed. Domestic economy is not comprised in buying a bad article for sixpence when better can be
got for sevenpence ; but if the ruling price for the best is eightpence, and somebody comes along and offers it for
sevenpence, then it’s domestic economy to close with the merchant so offering. By all means study this great
and glorious science of social life, because its due and proper observance cannot fail to make poor people rich, and
rich ones richer. What say you ? Will you do your business at

[?]
846 Macquarie-street North, Sydney,
January 24, 1894,
Lady Oarington is remembered with enthusiasm
at Katoomba, especially at the colliery in the
valley, where a miner pointed at an almost per-
pendicular deposit of burnt coal and said proudly,
“Lady Oarington has been twice down that there
ash ’eap ! ” How the dolly little woman negoti-
ated it, unless she slid down on the principle that
a boy applies to a bannister, I don’t know, but, at
all events, the legend is unshakeable, and I
couldn’t help thinking what some people would
have given for the ash Lady O. must have taken
out of her shoes when she got to the top.
Apropos of Lady Oarington, the very latest
about her from the other side of the pond presents
that pmk and white lady in a still more artificial
light than even we knew her by. Speaking of
some princess or other who recently paid a visit to
Somebody Quite as Mighty, a London paper said ;
“Her fresh, fair, wholesome colouring was quite
a contrast to an arrival immediately preceding
that of her Royal Highness—that of Lady Caring-
ton. Very golden locks and roseate cheeks are
natural in the twenties; but a score years later—
well, they do not look so at any rate.”
In Sydney “Lily’s” locks were the fawn or
mousey colour of a plaited sugar-bag, and though
the roseate cheeks bloomed miraculously right
through our anaemia-haunted climate, still the
hair remained as Nature intended, at any rate.
Gold-wash, capping all the top-dressing, must make
little Lady O. look like a ballet-girl who has
kicked her way into the peerage. But perhaps
the artful baron smoothes over the shock by ex-
plaining that the fierce Australian sun simply
made hay of Lily’s hair.
Apropos of vice-regalities, they say that one
Australian Governor freezes his more pushing
subjects per medium of a glass eye. But it strikes
me that a glass eye would be a distinct advantage
to any Governor. A potentate who can always
rely on a stony, unseeing glare in at least one
optic ought to have the pull over a ruler who has
to manufacture his own unspeakable expression on
the spot.
A new wrinkle came in with the summer—that
of named hat-bands on the “ sailor’’-shaped
toppings-off that every woman, who hasn’t a face
like a horse, now clings to. The idea meant to be
conveyed is, that the bands are the gifts of stricken
naval swains, and probably, in the first instance,
before the style had got into the shops, there was
nmething in it.
There is a certain amount of gratification—look-
ing at the vanity from a butterfly point of view-
in having the name of your particular Captain
Corcoran’s floating abode going all round your hat
in gold letters—especially when the ribbon is a
souvenir. But when it comes to running into a
shop and buying a small boy’s “sailor” with
“ Dreadnought,” or some such nautical old fogey,
described on it, and picking the band off to adorn
your own cranium, I must say I quite fail to see
the point of the thing.
But if women will wear christened headgear,
the least they can do is to hunt up something that
doesn’t fit them too accurately. For instance, I
met a regular acidulated drop of a female on the
Block recently in one of the branded hats, and
the name she was prancing under was “Spitfire.”
She was a far more congruous exhibition than the
hatchet-faced woman with the dog’s-eared mouth,
who scowls at you over a brooch with “Pet” on
it—but, still, I think that a prudent middle course
in naming yourself for public appreciation is un-
doubtedly the best policy. A dressy Sydney girl,
who does the Block under the title of “Rattler,”
has a far better idea of the possibilities of the
situation.
It is always a delicate operation deciding whom
“ baby ” favours for the benefit of the fond
mamma, who may or may not wish to have papa’s
Roman nose or her own poodle-pug declared to
preponderate in her cherub’s face. But when the
said papa is more or less unauthenticated, so to
speak, a realty wise person dandling the darling
astutely traces mamma or nobody in its blinking
visage.
Not so a gushing Thespienne, who was behaving
like a “giddy ox” (as “Charley’s Aunt”remarks)
over the small olive-branch of a certain charming
actress, who brought the infant behind the scenes
up North the other day. After various fulsome
ecstasies, the foolish virgin started on the pebbly
path—exceedingly pebbly under the circumstances
—of “Who is he like?” Gazing raptly into the
mottled visage, she said, in a voice of the most
intense conviction: “Isn’t it just like Mr.
Snooks!”
Now, if Mr. Snooks had been even a possible
interpretation the betise would have passed muster
as a kindness one woman may occasionally do
another in an unguarded moment—but, as Mr.
Snooks has been an unknown matrimonial quan-
tity for ever and ever so long, his was the very
last cap to fit on to the situation. It was as if
you started to congratulate a ten years’ widow-
woman on how much the precious two-year-old
resembled poor, dear Mr. Sorrel-top.
I have always thought that you want to be more
warily on your guard when a baby is the topic
than at almost any other social moment —the pit-
falls are so many and the graceful escapes so few.
Only the other day, in my hearing, a Sydney
lady was catechizing a N.S.W. politician on
the number of mouths in the nest. “ You have
17 children have you not, Mr. Dash ?” said she,
airily. “ No, madame,” was the reply, “ nOt 17 —
at least, my wife has not.”
Which recalls the anecdote —and all these
storyettes confirm me in my opinion that the
baby-topic requires diplomacy to handle properly
—of the Sydney man who met a matron of his
acquaintance and stopped to have a yarn. “So
you are married?” she began (it’s always a
woman who drags in the connubial question two
minutes after shaking hands with a man, by the
way). “ Yes? and how many children have you?”
“ Two—at home,” he responded majestically.
Then she fell back eagerly on the weather.
It is really one of the conundrums of the age
why theatre-managers don’t have a “hats-off”
rule for the stalls as well as the dress-circle, for
there is no manner of doubt that one woman in a
pancake hat can render the entertainment dull
and void for everybody in a line behind her. Only
the other evening, at “Charley’s Aunt,” I am told
that a woman sat in the front stalls, wearing one
of those flapdoodle roofings which are perched on
top of a pyramid of hair, and a man behind her,
worked up to aggravation pitch, sent her a diplo-
matic note, asking her to imagine she was in the
dress circle and take off her hat.
As a matter of course the “ lydy ” coldly
ignored the request, and sat with the tower-
shaped top - knot firmly pinned on and the
far-away gaze of a person whose heart is in the
Highlands ohas ; -.g deer for the rest of the enter-
tainment. Lut I don’t altogether blame her,
though I sympathise with her victims. Woman
is an “arranged ” individual who cannot always
take off her cranial fixings at the bidding of a
strange man, and though the spirit of the lady in
question may have been willing to oblige, the
dressing of her hair may have been weak. Before
the bitter cry of the hindermost stalls will be
hushed in the land a managerial “hats off” will
have to be pinned up and poor, weak woman (who
has put up with a lot of sarcasm over the hat
question) will have some sort of a precedent to
point to when she does the play from the stalls
bareheaded. I know the aghast expression
conventionally-bonnetted femininity aims at a
woman who wears her hat in her lap, and I don’t
wonder that possibly amiable but stereotyped
women prefer to do things like other people in the
absence of a rescuing byelaw.
Mrs. Essie Jenyns Wood has been fagged with
visitors since her return to Sydney. In fact, a
steady procession of admirers absolutely haunted
the Australia during her brief stay in Sydney,
keeping the ex-idol up to the small hours nearly
every evening.
I am told that Mrs. Wood (will Sydney ever
forget her wedding !) still considers “Jack” the
most delightful article in husbands who ever
backed away from an altar in double harness, and
that she is plumper than of yore. “Jack ” is as
much her theme of themes to-day as he was when
she approved of him so thoroughly to the London
reporters. “Jack is so good-tempered, you
know,” she told one Sydneyite after two minutes’
chat the other day.
Essie, by the way, has no present or future inten-
tion of returning to the stage, but describes herself
contentedly enough as “ occupying the humbler
position of a private citizen.” Which decision
doesn’t surprise me in the least, for even in the
zenith of her success Miss Essie, when among her
cronies, always candidly confessed a dislike to
her profession, which was more or less forced upon
her by Plain Bill Holloway. There is one Sydney
Johnnie who tells you with bated breath even
now that the best Australian Juliet once confided
to him that she “ hated Shakespeare!” Mr. and
Mrs. Wood return to the Hotel Australia in
March.
Society at the most popular of N.S.W. mountain
summer resorts is so chosen that the hotel’s pet
name is now “the Synagogue.” I think the
proprietor ought to advertise it that way if he
wants to puff it. One may always take the good
programme on trust wherever Israel sets the seal
of its approval.
The people who poke their alpenstocks in your
eye about Katoomba are not frantically mountain-
eering in appearance. The women topple about
the various slippery gorges in trimmed skirts and
lace-edged petticoats, and even evening shoes,
which suggest twisted ankles to the sympathetic
observer. I saw one girl negotiating a fall in
bow-toed slippers and four inches of white em-
broidery round her underskirt, which she was
clutching desperately out of the puddles together
with her upper garment—but all the same the
open-work was four inches deep in soil, and the
poor damsel looked as if she would give her jewel-
box to be able to prove an alibi on the spot.
Low as the Blue Mountains undoubtedly are
when compared with the world’s average, still
they are quite high enough to make knicker-
bockers and skirts to the knee a crying necessity
to the woman who wants to look presentable and
feel comfortable when careering on the mountain's
brow; and these Dorothy Draggletails towing
their sodden, fashionably-cut skirts over the
vertical face of nature, show how city-deep is the
Australian art of dressing yourself.
Oddly enough, there is an attempt at the proper
sort of thing from the waist up where it doesn’t
matter what you wear. You see mannish skirts,
neckties—(“ What a loud taste your women have
for scarfs!’’said a disgusted man to me—“you
rush for red neckties as if you were so many bull-
fighters ”)—sloppy coats, and tweed caps, but
sense swishes off into sagging multitudinous
draperies below the meridian, and the beautiful
promise of the upper-half of the Blue Mountain
girl is unfulfilled. And the saddest part of my
song is that the Draggle-tails will continue in-
definitely despite all shrieks—or at least they will
last until we stand on a different footing. The
typical Australian maiden’s large, shingle-shaped
tootsies do not make the fair owner’s fancy lightly
turn to thoughts of tucks.
The men are far more fittingly attired for the
mountains than the women, though I think one
English tourist overdid it. He had very pleated
breeches to the knee, giving the effect of a divided
skirt, thick stockings over un-Ryley-like shanks, a
nicely-fitting Norfolk jacket girded in at the waist
with a belt, and a small cap. He was a bean-pole
man and his clothes seemed to rise and obscure
the firmament—also his eye-glass was aggressive,
and he carried a weather-worn strong-box, sug-
gestive of the family jewels on tour, or the fatal
deeds that the villain is always swindling George
Rignold out of in the play. But all las glory
went out like a wooden match when at Penrith
(I pinned this specimen en route) he rushed from
the train and drank a deep draught from the
station filter immediately after an Asiatic
hawker 1 There is no beastly pride about a
globe-trotter when it lies between a free iron cup
and a sixpenny bar.
The Commodore of Prince Alfred Yacht Clubi
Mr. S. Hordern, gave an enjoyable garden party
to the members of the club at his town residence,
Darling Point, on Saturday afternoon. Mrs.
Hordern received her plethora of guests in the
arbor entrance to the lawn, and wore a quietly
stylish gown of sage-green mingled with lace
and insertion and bands of bengaline, small black-
jet bonnet trimmed with shaded pink roses, and an
ecru satin parasol covered with black lace.
There were a good many black gowns, all
necessarily handsome because the black toilet of
festivity must be of silks and laces and beads, or
else it recalls the freight of a mourning-coach.
Mrs. Alfred Bennett wore a smart white suit with
a pale-blue knot in a small black-jet bonnet; Mrs.
George Brock’s handsome toilet was white ottoman
silk, richly trimmed with Valenciennes lace and
insertion, small black jet bonnet with a yellow
flower; pretty Miss Booth sported a pinky frock
like the sartorial vision of a person looking
through rose-coloured specs., and Miss Dolly
Hordern and her small sisters wore white.
The members of the yacht club, headed by the
commodore, wore their brass-bound uniforms and
lent a quarter-deck aspect to the lawn, where two
bands rent the air with operatic melodies, and
various games came to the rescue of the non-
talkative. Lawn cricket was one of them, and a
most amusing incident was when an M.P., who
wasn’t voluntarily scouting, stopped a ball with
his glittering pot-hat. From the billiard-room
(where the comprehensive “afternoon tea” was
artistically set out) a good view of the harbour
with the club yachts, flag-decorated, at anchor,
was obtained, and, as the day was perfect, the
whole made up into a pretty picture.
Is it “ to meet the times,” I wonder, that Potts
Point now clutches at you with bare hands? It
is certainly “ the movement ” round about the
Points to wear your hands au naturel, but while
“ Society ” is alleged to be dormant, a body
cannot say for certain whether the somewhat plea-
sant summer fashion of naked fists will last or
not. In the off-season many eccentricities make
their appearance, only to fade sadly away when
the banner waves o’er Govmentous. I saw a girl
in the dress-circle at a Sydney show the other
evening with no finger-bags on, and .one or two
really splendid rings—of course. To flash the
jewels, the damsel sat chewing her nails medita-
tively from act to act, while the creeps seized me
at the spectacle. Imagine the aftermath! Dia-
monds like hailstones drawing everybody’s atten-
tion to spatula finger-tips!
The Bong Bong race-people are evidently deter-
mined to be bong tong. You would not be much
surprised to hear whom they pilled for their race-
club the other day.
About two years ago a politician presented a
church with an elegant American organ, which
was gratefully accepted by the local clergy. A
little while ago a van from a city music warehouse
was backed up to the church, and the organ
carried away. It appears the generous donor had
obtained the organ on the time-payment system,
and, having made default in the instalments, the
usual thing happened.
As “Beauty and the Beast,” at Sydney Lyceum,
nears the end of its wild career a person notices
that Nellie Stewart’s short black satin costume
looks like her Mabel dress dipped in ink—but I
don’t agree with the men who say at least
I am told they say that the cream robe
over flesh-pink tights, and a flesh-pink close-
fitting cuirass show more of Nellie Stewart
than the world has been privileged to gaze
upon before. As a matter of truth, the dress
is done decently and in order, but as unsophisti-
cated man probably mistakes the naturally-tinted
lining for cuticle, I don’t wonder he sits in amaze-
ment.
Also, one notices towards the end of the ’mime
that the seductive Bartho occasionally limps into
the wings when her enthralling teetotumisms are
over, and unpleasant memories of corns under the
toenails revive in the minds of adorers who follow
the last flutter of her tarletan skirt among the
butterflies. The other evening I thought Bartho
was going very dottily on her near side ankle as
she was walking up back, but she flew about the
stage in her active moments as daintily as what’s-
o’-clock in windy weather.
Place, fashionable suburban fancy-fair inTitipu.
Time, early in the year one. Lady Blank, wife of
the Governor of Titipu, opens the affair and is
presented with beautiful bouquet of white flowers.
Bouquet is afterwards found sticking in tree in
garden, and is used in one of the side-shows in the
evening. 3o much do “We” love flowers.
Same lady visits all the stalls; at one buys ss.
worth of goods, and tenders half-a-sov. in pay-
ment. Lady in charge of stall has no change.
“Never mind,’’ grandly says Lady Blank—“you
can send the change up to Govt. House.” Yet
she is the wife of a man with many “seats” at
’Orae! Tell me—how can such utterly tactless
people ever hope to succeed in Australia ?
Yours faithfully,
SAPPHO SMITH.
With this issue (728) The Bulletin com-
pletes its fourteenth year.
Dr. B. W. Richardson says that the time
is coming when all medicines will be inhaled, and
all disease will die away in an era of anaesthetics.
The Age has a pretty taste for simile.
Criticising the socialism of poet Morris :
. P a . r ents, being thus relieved from all household respon-
sibilities, are to form and dissolve their unions as freely
as dogs in the street.
She : “You abominable man? No flowers,
no reception, barely a pleasant word—and I have
been away eight weeks !” He ; “ You are right—
I am, indeed, an ungrateful wretch !”—Fliegende
Blatter.
“ ‘Oh, if you speak of some one as an
honest man,’said Miss Grantham, ‘it implies that
he’s nothing particular besides. I don’t mind a
little mild honesty, but it should be kept in the
background.’ ” — Dodo.
One Adelaide daily, after making heavy
reductions in its literary and general staff, has
plucked up courage enough to attempt slicing
the wages of the compositors. Reporters,
having no union, are easily dealt with.
The reply to Peel’s paper was, The Bul-
letin hears, written by T. A. Dibbs for presenta-
tion to the Bankers’ Institute, London. Premier
Dibbs heard of it, and published it, witli scarcely
a modification, as a N.S.W. State paper.
As of old, it takes a gold-rush to liven
things up. At Perth, W.A. a little party of
seventy, on a recent race night' put away 74
bottles of champagne and 40 bottles of whisky.
How much Worcester sauce was drunk next morn-
ing is not recorded.
The great Tiffany diamond, exhibited at
the World’s Pair, has been bought Mrs. Charles
T. Yerkes, the wife of the Chicago millionaiie,
for $lOO,OOO. Anyone named Yerkes should bo
entitled to be a pig-slaying millionaire, own a
wife with a big diamond, and hail from Porkvilte.
Queensland sugar output for last season is
reckoned at 79,650 tons : Cairns, 2000 tons; John-
stone, 7500; Herbert, 7400; Burdekin. 5550;
Mackay, 25,200; Rockhampton, 1000; Bunda-
berg, 24,000; Maryborough and Isis, 5400; and
Brisbane, 1500. Average per acre, 1 ton 15 cwt.
The R.O. church at Long Island city (New
York, U.S.A.) was recently burnt down. The
Baptist clergyman immediately offered the use of
his church to the bereaved priest. The offer was
gratefully accepted, and next Sunday two masses
were said in the Baptist church before the regular
service.
Adelaide City Corporation, wroth because
the P.M. has had the temerity to dismiss informa-
tions laid by its inspectors, demands that a Civic
Court shall be established. As the fines levied in
it would go to the Municipal authorities they
would then be prosecutors, judges and bene-
ficiaries.
They have been haggling in America over
how to pronounce the name of the Earl of Dun-
raven’s yacht. Says one paper; “The name of
a yacht, like the name of a man, is an arbitrary
thing, to be pronounced as Heaven and the owner
please; there is, in Virginia we believe, a family
who spell their name ‘Enroughty,’ and pro-
nounce it ‘ Darby.’ So Dunraven can call his boat
as he pleases.”
A N.S.W. draper’s assistant has just been
arrested for steiding. He was detected through
the constant alterations in his bulk caused by the
soft goods which he wrapped round him. History
repeats itself more or less. In Paris, some years
ago, a man was arrested by the octroi officers
because he looked too fat. When overhauled, he
was found to be wearing an indiarubber stomach
containing a couple of gallons of brandy.
Thus the Age on its Tory rival:—
It may boast the eyes of Argus and the hands of
Briareus ; but as long as they are enlisted in the slowly
dying cause of privilege and prescription, its activity,
like the activity of the grave-digger, will only make its
doom the surer, till from assuming the airs of Timotheus
“ placed on high,” it drops from its perch and com-
placently raises its cap to everybody who will “give pool
old Belisarius a penny."
All this because Belisarius called David’s paper
“ Ananias !’’
The manager of Walhalla (Quirindi,
N.S.W.) Squatting Co. left behind him four
signed blank cheques for use by the book-keeper
during his absence. The cheques were stolen and
one of them, filled up for <£l6oo, was successfully
cashed for gold in Sydney by a man not yet dis-
covered. The whole loss, of course, falls on the
company, not upon the bank. In like manner, a
Melb. bank was “had” some years back. The
casher of the cheque, it was believed, wore a false
beard when he presented it; he got gold and was
not again heard of,
Mr. P. Summers, manager of Australian
Reookd, writes to the Band of Mbeoy to say that
when a beast of burden refuses to move you should
not beat him, or stone him, or kick him, or light a
fire under him. You should take his ears gently
but firmly between finger and thumb, and turn
them inside out. Then the animal moves at once.
Now there is Barkes, an animal who has blocked
the course of Australian politics for nearly half-a-
oentury. He has been coaxed, threatened, politi-
cally bribed, implored ; but he persistently re-
fuses to move. Has anyone ever tried turning his
ears inside-out ?
One Wileman has, it is cabled, bobbed up
serenely in his coffin at Burton-on-Trent (Eng.',
and demanded a drink. As he had been dead for
three days, the sorrow of his surviving relatives is
easily to be imagined. When you have made a
fair divide of the property of a corpse, and
arranged that J ane is to have dear uncle’s house,
while Emma takes the furniture, and John the
cash, there are few minds to which the readjust-
ment of ideas and belongings involved in a resur-
rection does not come with a perceptible shock.
It is quite, certain that the relatives of even
Lazarus viewed his revival with very mixed feel-
ings, and were none too thankful for the miracle.

A Moral for Murderers.
(FOR THE BULLETIN.)
Jack Wilson was a stranger to our part of the
country, though it was easy to see that he had
passed most of his time somewhere in the bush.
He had been on the place about six months and
was now working as boundary-rider.
He and I never did hit it. Speaking honestly,
I don’t think he was a bad sort of fellow, barring
his queer temper, which I had roused once or
twice.
One Tuesday I was riding home from the next
station by a near cut through a rocky bit of coun-
try. I had just crossed a creek and climbed out
of a gully—the creek here makes a sharp bend
through our line and runs under a rocky point
into the adjacent property —when, right on the
point, outside the fence, I saw Wilson some 300
yards off.
You have often seen two fellows wrestling.
Well, Wilson was there seemingly wrestling for
his life, but wrestling with nothing visible!
Wrestling, apparently, with a phantom ! Grip-
ping thin air, grabbing and lifting and being
borne down by vacancy, swaying this way and
that way, digging his toes desperately in the
ground and trying to throw and crush and break
the back of—Nothing !
The struggle took place about three paces from
the edge of the point. Inch by inch Wilson gave
way before ids invisible foe; inch by inch he
fought to press back the empty air, but in vain ;
at last he touched the verge, his arms were flung
from him violently backwards and outwards, and
he fell headlong—hurled by Nothingness over the
point.
I rode home and said nothing.
Wilson’s horse was found in the paddock the
next day, and his body turned up in the creek on
Thursday.
I attended the inquest. I was a little curious
to hear if there was any suspicion of foul play, but
nothing was suggested nearer than suicide. They
gave the dead man the benefit of the doubt, and
brought in a verdict of accidental death.
I didn’t agree with the verdict, but, had I given
my evidence, they’d have probably hanged me or
sent me to an asylum.
OLD Q.
The Baffled Burglar.
(FOR THE BULLETIN.)
Vincent Smugo, M.L.0., of Ponsonby House,
Yanuka (S.A.), and Mrs. Vincent Smugg were at
loggerheads, after 15 years of domestic tranquility.
The cause was understood to be a pretty parlour-
maid.
Charles Muggins, burglar, had his professional
eyes on Ponsonby House. Having obtained, like
a prudent general, a plan of the interior fortifica-
tions, he selected as his point of assault a first-
floor window —one of two windows that belonged
to (1) a lumber-room and (2) the housemaid’s bed-
room. Muggins naturally preferred the lumber-
room.
Muggins mounted his ladder, slipped the
window-catch, raised the sash and climbed in,
dark-lantern (shut) in hand. Suddenly, he felt
himself seized by the collar and violently shaken,
while a female voice screeched —
“ Oh, you old brute ! Oh, you beast! You old
fiend ! I guessed this, and she’s away at her own
place for the night! To think that I should be
betrayed like this ! I, who’ve been a—fa-a-aithful
(sob) wi-ife (tears) to you ! You hypocrite!”
Muggins, petrified with dismay at this un-
expected variation of the programme, dropped the
dark-lantern in his struggle to slew round and get
away. The lantern-shutter slid back, and the
lantern gleamed its own commentary on the
scene.
Then Mrs. Smugg let go and shrieked, “Help!
Murder! Burglars ! Fire ! Water ! Help !
My God ! I thought it was Mr. Smugg!
Thieves ! Fire ! Murder !”
But Mr. Muggins was 300 yards away.
Mrs. Smugg repents now, with worshipping
humility, and says she can never forgive herself
for having misjudged Mr. Smugg.
Smugg is condescendingly forgiving.
The pretty parlour - maid smiles and says
nothing, but takes with simple alacrity all the
expiatory gifts which her mistress gives her. She
also, as usual, takes gifts from Mr. Vincent
Smugg.

UNTO DEATH.
(FOR THE BULLETIN.)
Medlow didn’t believe it!
Convinced of the ethnological oneness of Malay
and Dyak, he had not yet grasped the fact that,
whereas the average Malay is about as big a liar
as the average white man, the Dyak’s departures
from truth are always few and never unnecessary.
So, though—chiefly because he couldn’t remember
the Dyak expression—he didn’t tell Jasmi that
she lied, he shook his head, knocked the ashes out
of his pipe, and turned over to go to sleep again.
But here he failed. A saffron streak was already
putting edge to the dark forest-line in the east,
and far and near the air was tortured by the crow-
ing of innumerable cocks.
Cocks? Was not this the day when, for the
honour of a dozen villages, a thousand birds or so
would don the steel and do battle to the death ?
To which great fray had not*he, Medlow, been
invited by Grimbah himself? “Good little girl,
Jasmi, in'her way ; but, like all the rest of ’em, a
consummate liar! What grudge can Grimbah
have against me ? I’ve never done the old chap
any injury !”
But Grimbah—at that very moment mustering
his merry men—was of a very different opinion.
Troubled for many days with gruesome pains in
his cedar-coloured stomach, he had first anointed
that curvilinear receptacle with monkey’s fat, and
had then placed upon an ant-hill in the jungle, as
offering to Jirong, Lord of Death, a little tambok
full of mangosteens. This, Medlow, botanising,
found ; and ate—good, easy man !—the property
of the god ! Which awful act the Orang’s
watchers saw; and now, never again, until the
deadly sacrilege should be expiated, could Grim-
bah’s dignified stomach know its former ease !
And thus, when Medlow, in pyjamas and pipe,
strolled unconcernedly forth to view the prepara-
tions, he found on all sides, not the ready Dyak
cordiality but the shamefaced sulkiness of men
who have orders which they hate but must obey.
* * •
Up the narrow track—through palm and
fern and orchid—toiled Medlow, not unkindly
helped by the tireless Dyaks of his escort. No
use to question them, the white man knew, with
silent Matyag listening in the rear. Saturnine
Matyag, the Celebese—whom the Dyaks hated as
much as they feared him—spoke not a word till
from the teeming jungle they emerged at last upon
a bare and rooky hill, and Medlow, gazing up-
wards, wondered at the regularity of the slope.
“Momang Durat!” said the Bugi then, and
Medlow understood. They were on the crater of
the extinct volcano—a crater, he had heard, filled
almost to the brim with water.
• » *
A dreary scene, beneath a drizzling rain—
the crater-lake of Momang Durat ! The water,
black as ink, stretched quarter-mile across the
circle, with, in the midst, a peaked and rocky
islet —the apex of an inner cone. Upon the sum-
mit of this islet an ironwood post, set deep into
the stone, and, fast to the post with many a crossed
and re-crossed lashing, a man—Medlow! A
sacrifice to Jirong, for four-and-twenty awful
hours he had—as, with a ghastly gaiety he phrased
it to himself —“remained at his post I” Four-
and-twenty hours, too, of continuous rain, or long
ere this an equatorial sun and an uncovered head
would have made an end of things. But, instead
of being frizzled, he was chilled to the bone, and
his eyes were dim with weakness as they swept
the solitary shore—fast fading now into tho misty
Tho doomed man’s mind began to wander. Out
towards him, from the rocky wall, he thought a
giant waded. “Splash! splash!”—he heard the
mighty limbs displace the water. “Could this
be Jirong? Was there, after all, any truth in
such ”—but now the giant swam. Here came his
hideous head along the water—huge and black,
with one vast fiery eye ! His voice,. too ! Surely
for a giant, small and weak. Then, with the
sound, the cobwebs cleared from Medlow’s brain :
it was a human voice that hailed bim near at hand,
The thing upon the water splashed up close, and
to the white man’s side, with a gourd-lamp in her
hand, sprang—Jasmi !
# * *
Directly on Orion’s belt fell Medlow’s re-
opening eyes. He was again a boy at home,
looking up and wondering. Then beneath his
heavy head stirred Jasmi’s faithful arm, and, with
the sound of her voice, it all came back. the
ram had ceased, the moon and stars were out, and
as Medlow rose and saw the severed lashings scat-
tered round he felt that there was yet a door to
“I knew not until midday, Mediloa, whither
they had taken thee ; then straight away I came,
nor took off one of these!”—and Jasmi pointed
to her holiday adornments—the extra armlets, the
cuirass of agate bead-strings, the heavy brass-wire
coil about her shapely neck. “ Would a woman
of thine own people have done as much for thee,
Meddoa?”
But Medlow, patting the little brown face,
answered not. He knew that no white woman
could have done it—breasted, in six hours, 30
mdes of mountain, handicapped the while with a
dozen pounds of brass and a tambok of provisions.
“A float-skin also did I bring,” the girl said,
“ lest thou shoulds’t be too weak to swim. On it
I crossed over, myself,” and she pointed to the m-
fitted bullock-hide of which Medlow s disturbed
imagination had made a giant s head.
Medlow stretched his limbs and thumped his
circulation back. “Let us go, then!” he said.
<< p or Beni-Laut will I make—Kalampu is my
good friend ; and you, my brave little woman,
shall come with me.” Then, for the first time —
and £ be } asb i_the white man kissed poor, faithful
and glad was he afterwards of that kiss.
Q the sn n en water went the pair ;
h ’ n t ‘ he bu l loek . skin , the man holding by
C( * ner £ he swam . No la^p had Jasmi now .
“They know me for thy friend,” she said ; “and,
misgin y they may have followed. Best,
~ j s darkness '’’
ifc ; yas t dark e h> The faint moon
showed them> a 3 they neare( f fche shore, to half-a-
score of watching eyes. Medlow saw nothing, but
Jasmi, stepping gently to a narrow ledge, made
out the crouching forms—started—slipped—and
fell with echoing plunge into the hungry black
water. Medlow, grasping wildly, caught—and
held—a tiny hollow sphere of brass—a garnnong
from Jasmi’s bedang - skirt. Then, heedless of
his foes, he clambered on to theledgeand watched
the water.
Said Matyag’s croaking voice above him—
“ For what waitest thou, white man? Do women
swim, brass-laden, as was Jasmi? ”
The words fell cold upon the white man’s heart,
and, spent as he was, he would have dived. But
strong hands held him ; for, said Matyag : “End
to end, a hundred cocoa-palms would not touch
bottom here. With Jirong now is Jasmi! No
other victim needs he. Go therefore, white man’
whither thou wilt!”
* * »
Retund Medlow’s neck there hangs —the
dearest thing he owns—a little hollow sphere of
brass. No bigger than a hazel-nut —it holds a
world of vain regret!
ALEX. MONTGOMERY.
The Sting 1 of the Adder.
(FOR THE BULLETIN.)
What’s that? Sting in its tail? Shows what
you know about adders. Eh ? All right. Hold
hard, and I’ll tell you chaps a bit of a story.
When I was stationed at—never mind where,
a God-forgotten hole on the Lachlan —there was a
fellow named Jim Foy wanted for horse-stealing.
Jim was a card, and no mistake. Old Foy ruined
himself trying to make a gentleman of him, but it
was no go. He was a hardy, good-looking chap,
well-educated, and could talk as cleverly as a
parson; but he was cursed by every soul who
trusted him.
Well, I rode down to old Foy’s selection ; but
Jim got wind of what was on and “cleared” the
day before. After a while I picked up his tracks
at a slip-rail at the back of the 640-run. My
horse was a Government beast, and a beauty, for a
wonder —good for 50 miles without a sweat.
It had rained a trifle during the night, making
the trail quite easy. I followed the tracks over a
long stretch of the eternal white-box country, and
hung to them through a ticklish bit of brigalow
scrub. Then came another stretch of open forest,
mostly yellow-jacket, some more brigalow; then
across a bit of a dry gully, and I sighted my man
taking things very coolly, I thought. His horse
was feeding, and Jim was sitting down with his
back propped against a tree. As I came up he
never moved a muscle. I pulled out the bracelets
and made a show of my Colt, but he took no
notice. I could see a queer look on his face, and
the sweat was dripping down him.
“What’s it to be, Jim,” I said : “ are you going
to kick, or will you come quietly?” He never
took his eyes off the feet of his pants.
“Quiet, for God’s sake,” he whispered, “there’s
an adder in there !”
“ An adder 1” In a second I slid down from
the saddle and walked cautiously up. I could
only see about eight inches of the tail-end of the
dull, dirty-brown, stumpy body, but there was no
mistaking that the hideous reptile was there sure
enough, wriggling and twisting horribly.
“ He must have got there while I was having a
bit of a doze. He’s trying to work up the leg.
There’s a razor in my saddle pocket. Get it,
quick 1”
Jim was shivering like a leaf, and the sweat
kept pouring off him, but his voice was firm
enough. I admired the fellow’s nerve, for a bite
from an adder means death in no time.
I ran and got the razor. Very carefully he
drew the blade across the cloth, making a triangu-
lar hole about six inches long. Then, with his eye
fixed on the opening, he stretched out his hand to
me.
“I can see him,” he said. “Quick! your
pistol; it’s the only chance I’ve got. I’ll try and
blow his head off.”
I passed him the shooter. He drew a deep,
sighing breath as his long lean fingers closed
firmly round the butt, then jerked his arm quickly
round, and I found myself looking down the
black, ugly barrel of my own revolver. I was had
proper.
“Move an inch, d n you !’’ says Jim, “ and
I’ll blow your brains out.” I stood stock still,
and he lay back on the tree and looked at me.
“Dear friend,” says Jim, in a cold, sneering
sort of way, and putting on what we used to call
his boarding-school manners, “ pardon, I prithee,
this betrayal of such truly magnificent credulity.
(Keep still, curse you !) Such verdant simplicity
is indeed refreshing. It reminds me of an Irish
jackeroo I once saw climbing a kurrajong to look
for pears. Attention! worthy representative of
a majestic law, and bend thy listening ear. The
adder, bless your shiny boots and spotless pants,
is as harmless as you are. (Won’t you keep still !)
The perspiring terror you saw streaming down
my jaws was supplied from the water-bag.
Knowing, dear man, your remarkable ability as a
tracker, I was puzzled how to throw yon off the
scent. Fortune favours the brave—which is pos-
sibly the reason why you are unfortunate.
Excuse my little joke. (Do that again, and I’ll
shoot you as I would a sneaking dingo, blast you!)
About an hour ago, my horse nearly stepped on
this brute, and I was just going to rub him out
when the idea flashed on me, and I’ve been shep-
herding him with loving tenderness, awaiting
thee. When I heard the beat of your horse in the
brigalow, I crushed his head with the heel of my
boot. . You know the scriptural quotation, “Thou
shalt bruise his head and he shall bruise thy
heel.” Unfortunately for you, he failed to accom-
plish his half of the bruising contract. (Ah !
would you?) I owe you this explanation as I
wish to point out your utter incapacity as a thief-
catcher. Your tracking ability is merely instinct,
whereas the adder episode is, I flatter myself, an
indication of brains —yes, dear boy, brains. But
I must be going, though it is hard to tear myself
away from such pleasant company.”
He shook the adder on to the ground and sprang
to his feet.
“ Some credulous folk assert that his nibs has a
sting in the tail. You’ll be able from experience
to deny this foolish theory in future, eh? Mean-
while, I’ll trouble you to lead up the nags. I’ve
developed a roving propensity of late and the pro-
spect of ten years’ compulsory confinement has
made me somewhat restless. Now, hurry ! and
don’t try any shuffling with me or, by God, I’ll
put a dark-brown taste in your mouth.”
What could a fellow do? I’ve gob as much
pluck as the next man, but I’m not a fool. I
trotted up the horses, he mounted mine and led
his own, turned in the saddle to inform me that it
was about 25 miles back to town, recommended
salt and hot water as a remedy for blistered feet,
begged to be remembered to the inspector, ex-
tended his fingers from the point of his nose, and
cantered off. He’s never been heard of from that
day to this.
I lost my jacket and got fired out of the force.
Curse mo if they didn’t try to prosecute me for
assisting Jim to escape. Hard, wasn’t it !
d d hard !
So now you know something about the~stmg in
an adder’s tail. Who’s got the most tobacco?
BILLIE GRANT.

petition.

to be added for exchange.

The Last of Six
Tales of the Austral Tropics.
By ERNEST FAVENC.
With Prefatory Note by Rolf Boldrewood.
Opinions of the Press :
“ The volume takes its name from one of the sketches, * The Last of Six,’ though in point of merit they all stand pretty well on a par The
author, who is familiar with life in Northern Queensland, writes with vigour about the doings of the whites and the natives in these farawav
parts. A strong vein of mysticism runs through his narratives, and there are ghosts in almost every chapter, and for those who are fond of
realistic work the volume will prove attractive reading.”— S.M. Herald, July 8, 1893. 01
“ No writer has given the world a more vivid description of Australia and its wilderness than Ernest Favenc, and the secret of this is fw
he has, by many years experience of travel and bush life, acquired a knowledge of this continent which is possessed by scarcely any other writer
He, too, possesses great descriptive powers. Whatever he puts pen to is certain to be good. In the stories under notice he has given us some of
his best work. That they will be widely read is certain.”— Sydney Mail, July 8, 1893. 8 or
J Present with force and vividness, as well as with the accuracy arising from long contact and keen observation, striking pictures of
parts of Australia unfamiliar to most of its inhabitants ; they tell, sometimes with remarkable power, of incidents such as happen in wM
when human hearts are stirred by strong passions, and of weird imaginings that move the minds of lonely men ‘ The Last of Six ’is a teVriKt
tale of cannibalism treachery and revenge upon the dead, which in other hands might be gross and hideous, but which Mr. Favenc makes of
thrilling interest. The Haunt of the Jmkarras’ suggests Rider Haggard and his imitators to some extent, but is itself relieved fromfho

“THE BULLETIN.”
(Published at SYDNEY , N.S.W.)
The Pulque Weekly—Australia’s Vuuparell
Illustrated Newspaper.
“Tb.e Bulletin,”
Premier Australian Literary Journal,
Is the only paper which has a consistent and dis-
tinctive Australian National Policy.
The Bulletin favours—
A Republican Form of Government.
One Person, one Vote.
Complete Secularisation and Freedom of State
Education.
Reform of the Criminal Code and Prison
System.
A United Australia and Protection against
the World.
Australia for the Australians.—The cheap
Chinaman, the cheap nigger, and the
cheap European pauper to be absolutely
excluded.
A State Bank, the issue of bank-notes to be a
State monopoly.
The direct election of Ministers by Parlia-
ment, instead of Party Government or
rather Government by Contradiction.
Anew Parliamentary System—one House to
be elected by constituencies as at present;
the other to be chosen by the whole
country voting as one Constituency,
A Universal System of Compulsory Life
Insurance.
The entire Abolition of the Private Owner-
ship of Land.
The Referendum.
The Abolition of Titles of so-called “nobility. ’
The public eye rejects as uninteresting more
than half of what appears in the newspapers of
the day. It is only the remainder that is found
in The Bulletin.
The Bulletin is an aggressive Democratic
paper which strives to exclude from its artisti-
cally-condensed columns all matter that is not
of general human interest. The Bulletin was
started 13 years ago with no capital but brains,
and has become the representative Australian
journal, because it possesses a vitality lacked
by the countless news paper-ventures in which
Australian capitalists have unavailingly spent
large fortunes.
The Bulletin's conductors claim that every
progressive paper on the Australian continent has
more or less endeavoured to profit by its example
and follow its lines. The result of its powerful
influence and its unprecedented literary and com-
mercial success can at once be seen by comparing
the methods pursued by the Australian daily and
week!}- press prior to and immediately after th
first issue of The Bulletin.
From the date of The Bulletin's inaugural
publication its success was assured. It had at
once a dozen more or less servile imitators, many
of which even took, so far as was possible, its
name. They are now all dead, while The Bul-
letin continues the one phenomenal success in
Australian journalism, the one completely non-
local and distinctly universal Australasian news-
paper.
The Bulletin proprietary have spent some
thousands of pounds in fitting their buildings
with the latest appliances for high-class printing
and the production of illustrations, and they have
now issued The Bulletin in an entirely new
form—printed with new type of improved legi-
bility on the finest paper, and adorned with
sketches from the hands of the ablest and best-
paid newspaper artists Australia has ever seen.
The Bulletin is the only well-established Aus-
tralian journal which, throughout its career, has
been consistently Radical. It points with pride
to the fact that for years it stood alone among
secular newspapers in advocating the Irish
National Cause, and that it was the only Aus-
tralian journal which denounced the Soudan
Expedition from its inception.
The Bulletin is the beacon-fire of national
progress; it is the forceful summons to the
crusade against the monopolies in land, wealth,
power, and privilege—the direct cause of half the
misery which now alfiicts British humanity.
Against the clabns of avaricious clergy who claim
to monopolise salvation, against the dark despotism
of grasping plutocrats, and against the exercise of
any human authority which has not been granted
by the people over whom it is exercised, The
Bulletin will fearlessly and ruthlessly contend.
The Bulletin, unlike the ordinary weekly
journal, is not loaded with a weight of matter
transferred from the columns of a daily paper
issued from the same office. The Bulletin is
not connected with any other paper, and the
matter it contains is all specially prepared by its
own unequalled staff.
The Bulletin is the journal which, of all Aus-
tralian journals, is most frequently quoted in Eng-
land, America, and the colonies.
The Bulletin, which is the only paper sold
by runners in the streets of every Australasian
capital, is the most widely-circulated of all papers
published south of the equator, and is the only
Australasian publication which enjoys a popu-
larity unhampered by the political boundaries
that so closely restrict other journals to the
colonies in which they are printed.
The Bulletin's red cover is equally familiar
to the bushman of the Far North, the stockman
of Central Australia, the pearl-sheller of Torres
Straits, and the digger in the Maoriland
Ranges. A paper which is at once the most
popular city publication and the organ of the
intelligent bushman must indeed be broadly based.
Perhaps the strongest proof of The Bulletin’s
popularity is found in the unchallengeable fact—
easily ascertainable from any postal official—that,
of all journals issued in Australasia, The Bul-
letin is the one most frequently remailed from
one reader to another.
The Bulletin is going ahead faster than any
other paper, because its vast mass of original and
specially-written reading matter is the brightest
and best, its illustrations the cleverest and most
artistic, and its policy the boldest and most
straightforward ever submitted to the Australian
public.

per Annum.
